<p><b>Abu'l-Fath Jalal-ud-din Muhammad Akbar</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-10">[9]</a></sup> (25 October 1542<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birth_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-birth-5">[a]</a></sup> â 27 October 1605),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranicaonline1_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-iranicaonline1-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> popularly known as <b>Akbar the Great</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">Ø§Ú©Ø¨Ø± Ø§Ø¹Ø¸Ù</span></span> <small>Persian pronunciation:Â </small><span class="IPA" lang="fa-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Persian" title="Help:IPA/Persian">[akbarÉª azam]</a></span>), and also as <b>Akbar I</b> (<small>Persian pronunciation:Â </small><span class="IPA" lang="fa-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Persian" title="Help:IPA/Persian">[akbar]</a></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-time-17">[16]</a></sup> was the third <a href="/wiki/Mughal_emperors" title="Mughal emperors">Mughal emperor</a>, who reigned from 1556 to 1605. Akbar succeeded his father, <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a>, under a regent, <a href="/wiki/Bairam_Khan" title="Bairam Khan">Bairam Khan</a>, who helped the young emperor expand and consolidate Mughal domains in India.
</p><p>A strong personality and a successful general, Akbar gradually enlarged the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> to include much of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. His power and influence, however, extended over the entire subcontinent because of Mughal military, political, cultural, and economic dominance. To unify the vast Mughal state, Akbar established a centralised system of administration throughout his empire and adopted a policy of conciliating conquered rulers through marriage and diplomacy. To preserve peace and order in a religiously and culturally diverse empire, he adopted policies that won him the support of his non-Muslim subjects. Eschewing tribal bonds and Islamic state identity, Akbar strove to unite far-flung lands of his realm through loyalty, expressed through an <a href="/wiki/Indo-Persian_culture" title="Indo-Persian culture">Indo-Persian culture</a>, to himself as an emperor.
</p><p>Mughal India developed a strong and stable economy, leading to commercial expansion and greater patronage of culture. Akbar himself was a patron of art and culture. He was fond of literature, and created a library of over 24,000 volumes written in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kashmiri_language" title="Kashmiri language">Kashmiri</a>, staffed by many scholars, translators, artists, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calligraphers" title="Calligraphers">calligraphers</a>, scribes, bookbinders and readers. He did much of the cataloging himself through three main groupings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray,_Stuart_2009_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murray,_Stuart_2009-18">[17]</a></sup> Akbar also established the library of Fatehpur Sikri exclusively for women,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiegandDavis1994273_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiegandDavis1994273-19">[18]</a></sup> and he decreed that schools for the education of both Muslims and Hindus should be established throughout the realm. He also encouraged bookbinding to become a high art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray,_Stuart_2009_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Murray,_Stuart_2009-18">[17]</a></sup> Holy men of many faiths, poets, architects, and artisans adorned his court from all over the world for study and discussion. Akbar's courts at <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agra" title="Agra">Agra</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fatehpur_Sikri" title="Fatehpur Sikri">Fatehpur Sikri</a> became centres of the arts, letters, and learning. <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Renaissance" title="Timurid Renaissance">Timurid</a> and Perso-Islamic culture began to merge and blend with indigenous Indian elements, and a distinct Indo-Persian culture emerged characterized by Mughal style arts, <a href="/wiki/Mughal_painting" title="Mughal painting">painting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mughal_architecture" title="Mughal architecture">architecture</a>. Disillusioned with orthodox Islam and perhaps hoping to bring about religious unity within his empire, Akbar promulgated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Din-i-Ilahi" title="Din-i-Ilahi">Din-i-Ilahi</a>, a syncretic creed derived mainly from <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> as well as some parts of <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>.
</p><p>Akbar's reign significantly influenced the course of Indian history. During his rule, the Mughal Empire tripled in size and wealth. He created a powerful military system and instituted effective political and social reforms. By abolishing the <a href="/wiki/Jizya" title="Jizya">sectarian tax</a> on non-Muslims and appointing them to high civil and military posts, he was the first Mughal ruler to win the trust and loyalty of the native subjects. He had <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_literature" title="Sanskrit literature">Sanskrit literature</a> translated, participated in native festivals, realising that a stable empire depended on the co-operation and good-will of his subjects. Thus, the foundations for a multicultural empire under Mughal rule were laid during his reign. Akbar was succeeded as emperor by his son, Prince Salim, later known as <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span></h2>
<p>Defeated in battles at <a href="/wiki/Chausa" title="Chausa">Chausa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kannauj" title="Kannauj">Kannauj</a> in 1539 to 1541 by the forces of <a href="/wiki/Sher_Shah_Suri" title="Sher Shah Suri">Sher Shah Suri</a>, Mughal emperor <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a> fled westward to <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Multiple5_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Multiple5-20">[19]</a></sup> There he met and married the then 14-year-old <a href="/wiki/Hamida_Banu_Begum" title="Hamida Banu Begum">Hamida Banu Begum</a>, daughter of Shaikh Ali Akbar Jami, a <a href="/wiki/Persians" title="Persians">Persian</a> teacher of Humayun's younger brother <a href="/wiki/Hindal_Mirza" title="Hindal Mirza">Hindal Mirza</a>. Jalal ud-din Muhammad Akbar was born the next year on 25 October 1542<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birth_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-birth-5">[a]</a></sup> (the fifth day of <a href="/wiki/Rajab" title="Rajab">Rajab</a>, 949 <a href="/wiki/Hijri_year" title="Hijri year">AH</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">[13]</a></sup> at the <a href="/wiki/Umarkot_Fort" title="Umarkot Fort">Rajput Fortress</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amarkot" title="Amarkot">Amarkot</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rajputana" title="Rajputana">Rajputana</a> (in modern-day Sindh), where his parents had been given refuge by the local Hindu ruler Rana Prasad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Akbar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3060" data-file-width="1649" decoding="async" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Akbar.jpg/170px-Akbar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Akbar.jpg/255px-Akbar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Akbar.jpg/340px-Akbar.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Akbar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Akbar as a boy</div></div></div>
<p>During the extended period of Humayun's exile, Akbar was brought up in Kabul by the extended family of his paternal uncles, <a href="/wiki/Kamran_Mirza" title="Kamran Mirza">Kamran Mirza</a> and <a href="/wiki/Askari_Mirza" title="Askari Mirza">Askari Mirza</a>, and his aunts, in particular Kamran Mirza's wife. He spent his youth learning to hunt, run, and fight, making him a daring, powerful and brave warrior, but he never learned to read or write. This, however, did not hinder his search for knowledge as it is always said when he retired in the evening he would have someone read.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AknamaVolI_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-AknamaVolI-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> On 20 November 1551, Humayun's youngest brother, Hindal Mirza, died fighting in a battle against Kamran Mirza's forces. Upon hearing the news of his brother's death, Humayun was overwhelmed with grief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erskine_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erskine-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>About the time of nine-year-old Akbar's first appointment, as governor of <a href="/wiki/Ghazni_Province" title="Ghazni Province">Ghazni</a>, he married Hindal's daughter, <a href="/wiki/Ruqaiya_Sultan_Begum" title="Ruqaiya Sultan Begum">Ruqaiya Sultan Begum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sterling_Publishers_Pvt._Ltd_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sterling_Publishers_Pvt._Ltd-26">[25]</a></sup> Humayun conferred on the imperial couple all the wealth, army, and adherents of Hindal and Ghazni. One of Hindal's <i><a href="/wiki/Jagir" title="Jagir">jagir</a></i> was given to his nephew, Akbar, who was appointed as its viceroy and was also given the command of his uncle's army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-27">[26]</a></sup> Akbar's marriage with Ruqaiya was solemnized in <a href="/wiki/Jalandhar" title="Jalandhar">Jalandhar</a>, Punjab, when both of them were 14-years-old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly_2000_123,_272_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly_2000_123,_272-28">[27]</a></sup> She was his first wife and chief consort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sang-E-Meel_Pub_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sang-E-Meel_Pub-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thackston1999p437_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thackston1999p437-6">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the chaos over the succession of Sher Shah Suri's son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islam_Shah" title="Islam Shah">Islam Shah</a>, Humayun reconquered Delhi in 1555,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> leading an army partly provided by his Persian ally <a href="/wiki/Tahmasp_I" title="Tahmasp I">Tahmasp I</a>. A few months later, Humayun died. Akbar's guardian, <a href="/wiki/Bairam_Khan" title="Bairam Khan">Bairam Khan</a> concealed the death in order to prepare for Akbar's succession. Akbar succeeded Humayun on 14 February 1556,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-India_Today_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-India_Today-31">[30]</a></sup> while in the midst of a war against <a href="/wiki/Sikandar_Shah_Suri" title="Sikandar Shah Suri">Sikandar Shah</a> to reclaim the Mughal throne. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalanaur,_Punjab" title="Kalanaur, Punjab">Kalanaur, Punjab</a>, the 14-year-old Akbar was enthroned by Bairam Khan on a newly constructed platform, which still stands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> He was proclaimed <i>Shahanshah</i> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> for "King of Kings").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-India_Today_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-India_Today-31">[30]</a></sup> Bairam Khan ruled on his behalf until he came of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_campaigns">Military campaigns</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_innovations">Military innovations</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:India_in_1605.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1832" data-file-width="1248" decoding="async" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/India_in_1605.jpg/220px-India_in_1605.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/India_in_1605.jpg/330px-India_in_1605.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/India_in_1605.jpg/440px-India_in_1605.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:India_in_1605.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mughal Empire under Akbar's period (yellow)</div></div></div>
<p>Akbar had a record of unbeaten military campaigns that consolidated Mughal rule in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-India_Today_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-India_Today-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lal_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lal-35">[34]</a></sup> The basis of this military prowess and authority was Akbar's skilful structural and organisational calibration of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mughal_army" title="Mughal army">Mughal army</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kulke_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kulke-36">[35]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mansabdari" title="Mansabdari">Mansabdari</a> system in particular has been acclaimed for its role in upholding Mughal power in the time of Akbar. The system persisted with few changes down to the end of the Mughal Empire, but was progressively weakened under his successors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kulke_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kulke-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Organisational reforms were accompanied by innovations in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cannons" title="Cannons">cannons</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fortifications" title="Fortifications">fortifications</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/War_elephant" title="War elephant">use of elephants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lal_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lal-35">[34]</a></sup> Akbar also took an interest in <a href="/wiki/Matchlock" title="Matchlock">matchlocks</a> and effectively employed them during various conflicts. He sought the help of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a>, and also increasingly of Europeans, especially <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a> and Italians, in procuring firearms and artillery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schimmel_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schimmel-37">[36]</a></sup> Mughal firearms in the time of Akbar came to be far superior to anything that could be deployed by regional rulers, tributaries, or by zamindars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards1_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1-38">[37]</a></sup> Such was the impact of these weapons that Akbar's <a href="/wiki/Vizier" title="Vizier">Vizier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abu%27l-Fazl_ibn_Mubarak" title="Abu'l-Fazl ibn Mubarak">Abul Fazl</a>, once declared that "with the exception of Turkey, there is perhaps no country in which its guns has more means of securing the Government than [India]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elgood_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elgood-39">[38]</a></sup> The term "<a href="/wiki/Gunpowder_empires" title="Gunpowder empires">gunpowder empire</a>" has thus often been used by scholars and historians in analysing the success of the Mughals in India. Mughal power has been seen as owing to their mastery of the techniques of warfare, especially the use of firearms encouraged by Akbar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gommans_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gommans-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Struggle_for_North_India">Struggle for North India</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kaiser_Akbar_b%C3%A4ndigt_einen_Elefanten.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1353" data-file-width="921" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Kaiser_Akbar_b%C3%A4ndigt_einen_Elefanten.jpg/170px-Kaiser_Akbar_b%C3%A4ndigt_einen_Elefanten.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Kaiser_Akbar_b%C3%A4ndigt_einen_Elefanten.jpg/255px-Kaiser_Akbar_b%C3%A4ndigt_einen_Elefanten.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Kaiser_Akbar_b%C3%A4ndigt_einen_Elefanten.jpg/340px-Kaiser_Akbar_b%C3%A4ndigt_einen_Elefanten.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kaiser_Akbar_b%C3%A4ndigt_einen_Elefanten.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mughal Emperor Akbar training an elephant</div></div></div>
<p>Akbar's father Humayun had regained control of the <a href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Agra" title="Agra">Agra</a> with <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid</a> support, but even in these areas Mughal rule was precarious, and when the Surs reconquered Agra and Delhi following the death of Humayun, the fate of the boy emperor seemed uncertain. Akbar's minority and the lack of any possibility of military assistance from the Mughal stronghold of <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a>, which was in the throes of an invasion by the ruler of <a href="/wiki/Badakhshan" title="Badakhshan">Badakhshan</a> Prince Mirza Suleiman, aggravated the situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly1_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly1-41">[40]</a></sup> When his regent, <a href="/wiki/Bairam_Khan" title="Bairam Khan">Bairam Khan</a>, called a council of war to marshall the Mughal forces, none of Akbar's chieftains approved. Bairam Khan was ultimately able to prevail over the nobles, however, and it was decided that the Mughals would march against the strongest of the Sur rulers, <a href="/wiki/Sikandar_Shah_Suri" title="Sikandar Shah Suri">Sikandar Shah Suri</a>, in the Punjab. Delhi was left under the regency of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tardi_Baig_Khan" title="Tardi Baig Khan">Tardi Baig Khan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly1_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly1-41">[40]</a></sup> Sikandar Shah Suri, however, presented no major concern for Akbar,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> and avoided giving battle as the Mughal army approached.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The gravest threat came from <a href="/wiki/Hemu" title="Hemu">Hemu</a>, a minister and general of one of the Sur rulers, who had proclaimed himself Hindu emperor and expelled the Mughals from the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Gangetic_Plain" title="Indo-Gangetic Plain">Indo-Gangetic plains</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly1_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly1-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Urged by Bairam Khan, who re-marshalled the Mughal army before Hemu could consolidate his position, Akbar marched on Delhi to reclaim it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> His army, led by Bairam Khan, defeated Hemu and the Sur army on 5 November 1556 at the <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Panipat" title="Second Battle of Panipat">Second Battle of Panipat</a>, 50 miles (80Â km) north of Delhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Panipat_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Panipat-44">[43]</a></sup> Soon after the battle, Mughal forces occupied Delhi and then Agra. Akbar made a triumphant entry into Delhi, where he stayed for a month. Then he and Bairam Khan returned to Punjab to deal with Sikandar Shah, who had become active again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards2_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2-45">[44]</a></sup> In the next six months, the Mughals won another major battle against Sikander Shah Suri, who fled east to <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a>. Akbar and his forces occupied <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a> and then seized <a href="/wiki/Multan" title="Multan">Multan</a> in the Punjab. In 1558, Akbar took possession of <a href="/wiki/Ajmer" title="Ajmer">Ajmer</a>, the aperture to <a href="/wiki/Rajputana" title="Rajputana">Rajputana</a>, after the defeat and flight of its Muslim ruler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards2_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2-45">[44]</a></sup> The Mughals had also besieged and defeated the Sur forces in control of <a href="/wiki/Gwalior_Fort" title="Gwalior Fort">Gwalior Fort</a>, the greatest stronghold north of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narmada" title="Narmada">Narmada</a> river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards2_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Royal begums, along with the families of Mughal amirs, were finally brought over from Kabul to India at the time â according to Akbar's vizier, Abul Fazl, "so that men might become settled and be restrained in some measure from departing to a country to which they were accustomed".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly1_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly1-41">[40]</a></sup> Akbar had firmly declared his intentions that the Mughals were in India to stay. This was a far cry from the political settlements of his grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a>, and father, Humayun, both of whom had done little to indicate that they were anything but transient rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly1_41-4"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly1-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards2_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2-45">[44]</a></sup> However, Akbar methodically re-introduced a historical legacy of the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Renaissance" title="Timurid Renaissance">Timurid Renaissance</a> that his ancestors had left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_into_Central_India">Expansion into Central India</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prince_Akbar_and_Noblemen_Hawking,_Probably_Accompanied_by_His_Guardian_Bairam_Khan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1328" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Prince_Akbar_and_Noblemen_Hawking%2C_Probably_Accompanied_by_His_Guardian_Bairam_Khan.jpg/170px-Prince_Akbar_and_Noblemen_Hawking%2C_Probably_Accompanied_by_His_Guardian_Bairam_Khan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Prince_Akbar_and_Noblemen_Hawking%2C_Probably_Accompanied_by_His_Guardian_Bairam_Khan.jpg/255px-Prince_Akbar_and_Noblemen_Hawking%2C_Probably_Accompanied_by_His_Guardian_Bairam_Khan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Prince_Akbar_and_Noblemen_Hawking%2C_Probably_Accompanied_by_His_Guardian_Bairam_Khan.jpg/340px-Prince_Akbar_and_Noblemen_Hawking%2C_Probably_Accompanied_by_His_Guardian_Bairam_Khan.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prince_Akbar_and_Noblemen_Hawking,_Probably_Accompanied_by_His_Guardian_Bairam_Khan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Akbar hawking with Mughal chieftains and nobleman accompanied by his guardian Bairam Khan</div></div></div>
<p>By 1559, the Mughals had launched a drive to the south into Rajputana and <a href="/wiki/Malwa_Sultanate" title="Malwa Sultanate">Malwa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards3_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richards3-47">[46]</a></sup> However, Akbar's disputes with his regent, Bairam Khan, temporarily put an end to the expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards3_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Richards3-47">[46]</a></sup> The young emperor, at the age of eighteen, wanted to take a more active part in managing affairs. Urged on by his foster mother, <a href="/wiki/Maham_Anga" title="Maham Anga">Maham Anga</a>, and his relatives, Akbar decided to dispense with the services of Bairam Khan. After yet another dispute at court, Akbar finally dismissed Bairam Khan in the spring of 1560 and ordered him to leave on <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-expansion_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-expansion-48">[47]</a></sup> Bairam Khan left for Mecca but on his way was goaded by his opponents to rebel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Panipat_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Panipat-44">[43]</a></sup> He was defeated by the Mughal army in the Punjab and forced to submit. Akbar forgave him, however, and gave him the option of either continuing in his court or resuming his pilgrimage; Bairam chose the latter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> Bairam Khan was later assassinated on his way to Mecca, allegedly by an Afghan with a personal vendetta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards3_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-Richards3-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1560, Akbar resumed military operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards3_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-Richards3-47">[46]</a></sup> A Mughal army under the command of his foster brother, <a href="/wiki/Adham_Khan" title="Adham Khan">Adham Khan</a>, and a Mughal commander, Pir Muhammad Khan, began the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_conquest_of_Malwa" title="Mughal conquest of Malwa">Mughal conquest of Malwa</a>. The Afghan ruler, <a href="/wiki/Baz_Bahadur" title="Baz Bahadur">Baz Bahadur</a>, was defeated at the Battle of Sarangpur and fled to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Khandesh" title="Sultanate of Khandesh">Khandesh</a> for refuge leaving behind his harem, treasure, and war elephants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards3_47-4"><a href="#cite_note-Richards3-47">[46]</a></sup> Despite initial success, the campaign proved a disaster from Akbar's point of view. His foster brother retained all the spoils and followed through with the Central Asian practice of slaughtering the surrendered garrison, their wives and children, and many Muslim theologians and Sayyids, who were the descendants of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards3_47-5"><a href="#cite_note-Richards3-47">[46]</a></sup> Akbar personally rode to Malwa to confront Adham Khan and relieve him of command. Pir Muhammad Khan was then sent in pursuit of Baz Bahadur but was beaten back by the alliance of the rulers of Khandesh and <a href="/wiki/Berar_Sultanate" title="Berar Sultanate">Berar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards3_47-6"><a href="#cite_note-Richards3-47">[46]</a></sup> Baz Bahadur temporarily regained control of Malwa until, in the next year, Akbar sent another Mughal army to invade and annex the kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards3_47-7"><a href="#cite_note-Richards3-47">[46]</a></sup> Malwa became a province of the nascent imperial administration of Akbar's regime. Baz Bahadur survived as a refugee at various courts until, eight years later in 1570, he took service under Akbar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards3_47-8"><a href="#cite_note-Richards3-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_Abdul_Rahim_Khan-I-Khana_being_received_by_Akbar,_Akbarnama.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="699" data-file-width="421" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Young_Abdul_Rahim_Khan-I-Khana_being_received_by_Akbar%2C_Akbarnama.jpg/170px-Young_Abdul_Rahim_Khan-I-Khana_being_received_by_Akbar%2C_Akbarnama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Young_Abdul_Rahim_Khan-I-Khana_being_received_by_Akbar%2C_Akbarnama.jpg/255px-Young_Abdul_Rahim_Khan-I-Khana_being_received_by_Akbar%2C_Akbarnama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Young_Abdul_Rahim_Khan-I-Khana_being_received_by_Akbar%2C_Akbarnama.jpg/340px-Young_Abdul_Rahim_Khan-I-Khana_being_received_by_Akbar%2C_Akbarnama.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_Abdul_Rahim_Khan-I-Khana_being_received_by_Akbar,_Akbarnama.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdul_Rahim_Khan-I-Khana" title="Abdul Rahim Khan-I-Khana">Abdul Rahim Khan-I-Khana</a> son of <a href="/wiki/Bairam_Khan" title="Bairam Khan">Bairam Khan</a> being received by Akbar</div></div></div>
<p>Despite the ultimate success in Malwa, the conflict exposed cracks in Akbar's personal relationships with his relatives and Mughal nobles. When Adham Khan confronted Akbar following another dispute in 1562, he was struck down by the emperor and thrown from a terrace into the palace courtyard at Agra. Still alive, Adham Khan was dragged up and thrown to the courtyard once again by Akbar to ensure his death. Akbar now sought to eliminate the threat of over-mighty subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards3_47-9"><a href="#cite_note-Richards3-47">[46]</a></sup> He created specialised ministerial posts relating to imperial governance; no member of the Mughal nobility was to have unquestioned pre-eminence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards3_47-10"><a href="#cite_note-Richards3-47">[46]</a></sup> When a powerful clan of Uzbek chiefs broke out in rebellion in 1564, Akbar decisively defeated and routed them in Malwa and then <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2-50">[49]</a></sup> He pardoned the rebellious leaders, hoping to conciliate them, but they rebelled again, so Akbar had to quell their uprising a second time. Following a third revolt with the proclamation of <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Muhammad_Hakim" title="Mirza Muhammad Hakim">Mirza Muhammad Hakim</a>, Akbar's brother and the Mughal ruler of Kabul, as emperor, his patience was finally exhausted. Several Uzbek chieftains were subsequently slain and the rebel leaders trampled to death under elephants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2-50">[49]</a></sup> Simultaneously the Mirzas, a group of Akbar's distant cousins who held important fiefs near Agra, had also risen up in rebellion. They too were slain and driven out of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2-50">[49]</a></sup> In 1566, Akbar moved to meet the forces of his brother, Muhammad Hakim, who had marched into the Punjab with dreams of seizing the imperial throne. Following a brief confrontation, however, Muhammad Hakim accepted Akbar's supremacy and retreated back to Kabul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1564, Mughal forces began the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_conquest_of_Garha" title="Mughal conquest of Garha">conquest of Garha</a>, a thinly populated, hilly area in central India that was of interest to the Mughals because of its herd of wild elephants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards4_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richards4-51">[50]</a></sup> The territory was ruled over by Raja Vir Narayan, a minor, and his mother, <a href="/wiki/Rani_Durgavati" title="Rani Durgavati">Durgavati</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Rajput" title="Rajput">Rajput</a> warrior queen of the Gonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2_50-4"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2-50">[49]</a></sup> Akbar did not personally lead the campaign because he was preoccupied with the Uzbek rebellion, leaving the expedition in the hands of Asaf Khan, the Mughal governor of Kara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2_50-5"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2-50">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra-52">[51]</a></sup> Durgavati committed suicide after her defeat at the Battle of Damoh, while Raja Vir Narayan was slain at the Fall of Chauragarh, the mountain fortress of the Gonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra-52">[51]</a></sup> The Mughals seized immense wealth, an uncalculated amount of gold and silver, jewels and 1000 elephants. Kamala Devi, a younger sister of Durgavati, was sent to the Mughal harem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra-52">[51]</a></sup> The brother of Durgavati's deceased husband was installed as the Mughal administrator of the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra-52">[51]</a></sup> Like in Malwa, however, Akbar entered into a dispute with his vassals over the conquest of Gondwana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra-52">[51]</a></sup> Asaf Khan was accused of keeping most of the treasures and sending back only 200 elephants to Akbar. When summoned to give accounts, he fled Gondwana. He went first to the Uzbeks, then returned to Gondwana where he was pursued by Mughal forces. Finally, he submitted and Akbar restored him to his previous position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra_52-5"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Attempt_to_murder_Akbar">Attempt to murder Akbar</span></h4>
<p>Around 1564 is also when there was an assassination attempt on Akbar documented in a painting. The attempt was made when Akbar was returning from a visit to the dargah of Hazrat Nizamuddin near Delhi, by an assassin shooting an arrow. The arrow pierced his right shoulder. The assassin was apprehended and ordered beheaded by the Emperor. The culprit was a slave of Mirza Sharfuddin, a noble in Akbar's court whose rebellion had recently been curbed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conquest_of_Rajputana">Conquest of Rajputana</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Akbar_shoots_Jaimal_at_the_siege_of_Chitor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="1673" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Akbar_shoots_Jaimal_at_the_siege_of_Chitor.jpg/170px-Akbar_shoots_Jaimal_at_the_siege_of_Chitor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Akbar_shoots_Jaimal_at_the_siege_of_Chitor.jpg/255px-Akbar_shoots_Jaimal_at_the_siege_of_Chitor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Akbar_shoots_Jaimal_at_the_siege_of_Chitor.jpg/340px-Akbar_shoots_Jaimal_at_the_siege_of_Chitor.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Akbar_shoots_Jaimal_at_the_siege_of_Chitor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mughal Emperor Akbar shoots the Rajput warrior Jaimal during the Siege of Chittorgarh in 1568</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bullocks_dragging_siege-guns_up_hill_during_the_attack_on_Ranthambhor_Fort.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="1602" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Bullocks_dragging_siege-guns_up_hill_during_the_attack_on_Ranthambhor_Fort.jpg/170px-Bullocks_dragging_siege-guns_up_hill_during_the_attack_on_Ranthambhor_Fort.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Bullocks_dragging_siege-guns_up_hill_during_the_attack_on_Ranthambhor_Fort.jpg/255px-Bullocks_dragging_siege-guns_up_hill_during_the_attack_on_Ranthambhor_Fort.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Bullocks_dragging_siege-guns_up_hill_during_the_attack_on_Ranthambhor_Fort.jpg/340px-Bullocks_dragging_siege-guns_up_hill_during_the_attack_on_Ranthambhor_Fort.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bullocks_dragging_siege-guns_up_hill_during_the_attack_on_Ranthambhor_Fort.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bullocks dragging siege-guns uphill during Akbar's attack on Ranthambhor Fort in 1568</div></div></div>
<p>Having established Mughal rule over northern India, Akbar turned his attention to the conquest of <a href="/wiki/Rajputana" title="Rajputana">Rajputana</a>. No imperial power in India based on the Indo-Gangetic plains could be secure if a rival centre of power existed on its flank in Rajputana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra_52-6"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra-52">[51]</a></sup> The Mughals had already established domination over parts of northern Rajputana in <a href="/wiki/Mewar" title="Mewar">Mewat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ajmer" title="Ajmer">Ajmer</a>, and Nagor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards2_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2_50-6"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2-50">[49]</a></sup> Now, Akbar was determined to drive into the heartlands of the <a href="/wiki/Rajput" title="Rajput">Rajput</a> kings that had rarely previously submitted to the Muslim rulers of the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a>. Beginning in 1561, the Mughals actively engaged the Rajputs in warfare and diplomacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards4_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Richards4-51">[50]</a></sup> Most Rajput states accepted Akbar's suzerainty; the rulers of Mewar and Marwar, <a href="/wiki/Udai_Singh_II" title="Udai Singh II">Udai Singh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chandrasen_Rathore" title="Chandrasen Rathore">Chandrasen Rathore</a>, however, remained outside the imperial fold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2_50-7"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2-50">[49]</a></sup> Rana Udai Singh was descended from the Sisodia ruler, <a href="/wiki/Rana_Sanga" title="Rana Sanga">Rana Sanga</a>, who had fought Babur at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Khanwa" title="Battle of Khanwa">Battle of Khanwa</a> in 1527.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2_50-8"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2-50">[49]</a></sup> As the head of the Sisodia clan, he possessed the highest ritual status of all the Rajput kings and chieftains in India.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Unless Udai Singh was reduced to submission, the imperial authority of the Mughals would be lessened in Rajput eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2_50-9"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2-50">[49]</a></sup> Furthermore, Akbar, at this early period, was still enthusiastically devoted to the cause of Islam and sought to impress the superiority of his faith over the most prestigious warriors in Brahminical Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly2_50-10"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1567, Akbar moved to reduce the <a href="/wiki/Chittor_Fort" title="Chittor Fort">Chittor Fort</a> in Mewar. The fortress-capital of Mewar was of great strategic importance as it lay on the shortest route from Agra to <a href="/wiki/Gujarat_Sultanate" title="Gujarat Sultanate">Gujarat</a> and was also considered a key to holding the interior parts of Rajputana. Udai Singh retired to the hills of Mewar, leaving two Rajput warriors, <a href="/wiki/Jaimal_Rathore" title="Jaimal Rathore">Jaimal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patta_Sisodia" title="Patta Sisodia">Patta</a>, in charge of the defence of his capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earlyconquest_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-earlyconquest-54">[53]</a></sup> Chittorgarh fell in February 1568 after a <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Chittorgarh_(1567%E2%80%931568)" title="Siege of Chittorgarh (1567â1568)">siege of four months</a>. Akbar had the surviving defenders and 30,000 non-combatants massacred and their heads displayed upon towers erected throughout the region, in order to demonstrate his authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chittor_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-chittor-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> The booty that fell into the hands of the Mughals was distributed throughout the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> He remained in Chittorgarh for three days, then returned to Agra, where to commemorate the victory, he set up, at the gates of his fort, statues of Jaimal and Patta mounted on elephants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly3_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly3-58">[57]</a></sup> Udai Singh's power and influence was broken. He never again ventured out his mountain refuge in Mewar and Akbar was content to let him be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly4_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly4-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>The fall of Chittorgarh was followed up by a Mughal attack on the <a href="/wiki/Ranthambore_Fort" title="Ranthambore Fort">Ranthambore Fort</a> in 1568. Ranthambore was held by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hada_(clan)" title="Hada (clan)">Hada</a> Rajputs and reputed to be the most powerful fortress in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly4_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly4-59">[58]</a></sup> However, it fell only after a couple of months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly4_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly4-59">[58]</a></sup> Akbar was now the master of almost the whole of Rajputana. Most of the Rajput kings had submitted to the Mughals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly4_59-3"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly4-59">[58]</a></sup> Only the clans of Mewar continued to resist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly4_59-4"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly4-59">[58]</a></sup> Udai Singh's son and successor, <a href="/wiki/Maharana_Pratap" title="Maharana Pratap">Pratap Singh</a>, was later defeated by the Mughals at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Haldighati" title="Battle of Haldighati">Battle of Haldighati</a> in 1576.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly4_59-5"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly4-59">[58]</a></sup> Akbar would celebrate his conquest of Rajputana by laying the foundation of a new capital, 23 miles (37Â km) W.S.W of Agra in 1569. It was called <a href="/wiki/Fatehpur_Sikri" title="Fatehpur Sikri">Fatehpur Sikri</a> ("the city of victory").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> Rana Pratap Singh, however, continuously attacked Mughals and was able to retain most of the kingdom of his ancestors in the life of Akbar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Annexation_of_Western_and_Eastern_India">Annexation of Western and Eastern India</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Court_of_Akbar_from_Akbarnama.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="380" decoding="async" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Court_of_Akbar_from_Akbarnama.jpg/170px-Court_of_Akbar_from_Akbarnama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Court_of_Akbar_from_Akbarnama.jpg/255px-Court_of_Akbar_from_Akbarnama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Court_of_Akbar_from_Akbarnama.jpg/340px-Court_of_Akbar_from_Akbarnama.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Court_of_Akbar_from_Akbarnama.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The court of young Akbar, age 13, showing his first imperial act: the arrest of an unruly courtier, who was once a favourite of Akbar's father. Illustration from a manuscript of the Akbarnama</div></div></div>
<p>Akbar's next military objectives were the conquest of Gujarat and Bengal, which connected India with the trading centres of Asia, Africa, and Europe through the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly4_59-6"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly4-59">[58]</a></sup> Furthermore, Gujarat had been a haven for rebellious Mughal nobles, while in Bengal, the Afghans still held considerable influence under their ruler, <a href="/wiki/Sulaiman_Khan_Karrani" title="Sulaiman Khan Karrani">Sulaiman Khan Karrani</a>. Akbar first moved against Gujarat, which lay in the crook of the Mughal provinces of Rajputana and Malwa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly4_59-7"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly4-59">[58]</a></sup> Gujarat, with its coastal regions, possessed areas of rich agricultural production in its central plain, an impressive output of textiles and other industrial goods, and the busiest seaports of India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly4_59-8"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly4-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gujarat1_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gujarat1-62">[61]</a></sup> Akbar intended to link the maritime state with the massive resources of the Indo-Gangetic plains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards5_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richards5-63">[62]</a></sup> However, the ostensible casus belli was that the rebel Mirzas, who had previously been driven out of India, were now operating out of a base in southern Gujarat. Moreover, Akbar had received invitations from cliques in Gujarat to oust the reigning king, which served as justification for his military expedition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly4_59-9"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly4-59">[58]</a></sup> In 1572, he moved to occupy <a href="/wiki/Ahmedabad" title="Ahmedabad">Ahmedabad</a>, the capital, and other northern cities, and was proclaimed the lawful sovereign of Gujarat. By 1573, he had driven out the Mirzas who, after offering token resistance, fled for refuge in the <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Plateau" title="Deccan Plateau">Deccan</a>. <a href="/wiki/Surat" title="Surat">Surat</a>, the commercial capital of the region and other coastal cities soon capitulated to the Mughals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly4_59-10"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly4-59">[58]</a></sup> The king, <a href="/wiki/Muzaffar_Shah_III" title="Muzaffar Shah III">Muzaffar Shah III</a>, was caught hiding in a corn field; he was pensioned off by Akbar with a small allowance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly4_59-11"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly4-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Having established his authority over Gujarat, Akbar returned to Fatehpur Sikiri, where he built the <a href="/wiki/Buland_Darwaza" title="Buland Darwaza">Buland Darwaza</a> to commemorate his victories, but a rebellion by Afghan nobles supported by the Rajput ruler of <a href="/wiki/Idar_State" title="Idar State">Idar</a>, and the renewed intrigues of the Mirzas forced his return to Gujarat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards5_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Richards5-63">[62]</a></sup> Akbar crossed the Rajputana and reached Ahmedabad in eleven days â a journey that normally took six weeks. The outnumbered Mughal army then won a decisive victory on September 2, 1573. Akbar slew the rebel leaders and erected a tower out of their severed heads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly4_59-12"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly4-59">[58]</a></sup> The conquest and subjugation of Gujarat proved highly profitable for the Mughals; the territory yielded a revenue of more than five million rupees annually to Akbar's treasury, after expenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly4_59-13"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly4-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Akbar had now defeated most of the Afghan remnants in India. The only centre of Afghan power was now in Bengal, where Sulaiman Khan Karrani, an Afghan chieftain whose family had served under Sher Shah Suri, was reigning in power. While Sulaiman Khan scrupulously avoided giving offence to Akbar, his son, <a href="/wiki/Daud_Khan_Karrani" title="Daud Khan Karrani">Daud Khan</a>, who had succeeded him in 1572, decided otherwise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly5_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly5-64">[63]</a></sup> Whereas Sulaiman Khan had the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khutba" title="Khutba">khutba</a> read in Akbar's name and acknowledged Mughal supremacy, Daud Khan assumed the insignia of royalty and ordered the khutba to be proclaimed in his own name in defiance of Akbar. <a href="/wiki/Munim_Khan" title="Munim Khan">Munim Khan</a>, the Mughal governor of Bihar, was ordered to chastise Daud Khan, but later, Akbar himself set out to Bengal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly5_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly5-64">[63]</a></sup> This was an opportunity to bring the trade in the east under Mughal control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pletcher_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pletcher-65">[64]</a></sup> In 1574, the Mughals seized <a href="/wiki/Patna" title="Patna">Patna</a> from Daud Khan, who fled to Bengal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly5_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly5-64">[63]</a></sup> Akbar returned to Fatehpur Sikri and left his generals to finish the campaign. The Mughal army was subsequently victorious at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tukaroi" title="Battle of Tukaroi">Battle of Tukaroi</a> in 1575, which led to the annexation of Bengal and parts of Bihar that had been under the dominion of Daud Khan. Only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orissa,_India" title="Orissa, India">Orissa</a> was left in the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Karrani_dynasty" title="Karrani dynasty">Karrani dynasty</a> as a fief of the Mughal Empire. A year later, however, Daud Khan rebelled and attempted to regain Bengal. He was defeated by the Mughal general, <a href="/wiki/Khan_Jahan_I" title="Khan Jahan I">Khan Jahan Quli</a>, and had to flee into exile. Daud Khan was later captured and executed by Mughal forces. His severed head was sent to Akbar, while his limbs were gibbeted at Tandah, the Mughal capital in Bengal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly5_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly5-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaigns_in_Afghanistan_and_Central_Asia">Campaigns in Afghanistan and Central Asia</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Akbar%27s_conquest_of_Gujarat" title="Akbar's conquest of Gujarat">Akbar's conquest of Gujarat</a></div>
<p>Following his conquests of Gujarat and Bengal, Akbar was preoccupied with domestic concerns. He did not leave Fatehpur Sikri on a military campaign until 1581, when Punjab was again invaded by his brother, Mirza Muhammad Hakim. Akbar expelled his brother to Kabul and this time pressed on, determined to end the threat from Muhammad Hakim once and for all. In contrast to the problem that his predecessors once had in getting Mughal nobles to stay on in India, the problem now was to get them to leave India. They were, according to Abul Fazl "afraid of the cold of Afghanistan." The Hindu officers, in turn, were additionally inhibited by the traditional <a href="/wiki/Kala_pani_(taboo)" title="Kala pani (taboo)">taboo against crossing the Indus</a>. Akbar, however, spurred them on. The soldiers were provided with pay eight months in advance. In August 1581, Akbar seized Kabul and took up residence at <a href="/wiki/Bala_Hissar,_Kabul" title="Bala Hissar, Kabul">Babur's old citadel</a>. He stayed there for three weeks, in the absence of his brother, who had fled into the mountains. Akbar left Kabul in the hands of his sister, <a href="/wiki/Bakht-un-Nissa_Begum" title="Bakht-un-Nissa Begum">Bakht-un-Nissa Begum</a>, and returned to India. He pardoned his brother, who took up de facto charge of the Mughal administration in Kabul; Bakht-un-Nissa continued to be the official governor. A few years later, in 1585, Muhammad Hakim died and Kabul passed into the hands of Akbar once again. It was officially incorporated as a province of the Mughal Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly5_64-4"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly5-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Kabul expedition was the beginning of a long period of activity over the northern frontiers of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Age_of_Akbar_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Age_of_Akbar-66">[65]</a></sup> For thirteen years, beginning in 1585, Akbar remained in the north, shifting his capital to Lahore in the Punjab while dealing with challenges from beyond the Khyber Pass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Age_of_Akbar_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Age_of_Akbar-66">[65]</a></sup> The gravest threat came from the <a href="/wiki/Uzbeks" title="Uzbeks">Uzbeks</a>, the tribe that had driven his grandfather, Babur, out of Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly5_64-5"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly5-64">[63]</a></sup> They had been organised under <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_Khan_II" title="Abdullah Khan II">Abdullah Khan Shaybanid</a>, a capable military chieftain who had seized Badakhshan and Balkh from Akbar's distant Timurid relatives, and whose Uzbek troops now posed a serious challenge to the northwestern frontiers of the Mughal Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly5_64-6"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly5-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dani_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dani-67">[66]</a></sup> The Afghan tribes on the border were also restless, partly on account of the hostility of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yusufzai_(Pashtun_tribe)" title="Yusufzai (Pashtun tribe)">Yusufzai</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bajaur" title="Bajaur">Bajaur</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swat_Valley" title="Swat Valley">Swat</a>, and partly owing to the activity of a new religious leader, Bayazid, the founder of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roshaniyya" title="Roshaniyya">Roshaniyya</a> sect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Age_of_Akbar_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-The_Age_of_Akbar-66">[65]</a></sup> The Uzbeks were also known to be subsidising Afghans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards6_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richards6-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1586, Akbar negotiated a pact with Abdullah Khan in which the Mughals agreed to remain neutral during the Uzbek invasion of Safavid held <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards6_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Richards6-68">[67]</a></sup> In return, Abdullah Khan agreed to refrain from supporting, subsidising, or offering refuge to the Afghan tribes hostile to the Mughals. Thus freed, Akbar began a series of campaigns to pacify the Yusufzais and other rebels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards6_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-Richards6-68">[67]</a></sup> Akbar ordered Zain Khan to lead an expedition against the Afghan tribes. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raja_Birbal" title="Raja Birbal">Raja Birbal</a>, a renowned minister in Akbar's court, was also given military command. The expedition turned out to be a disaster, and on its retreat from the mountains, Birbal and his entourage were ambushed and killed by the Afghans at the Malandarai Pass in February 1586.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards6_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-Richards6-68">[67]</a></sup> Akbar immediately fielded new armies to reinvade the Yusufzai lands under the command of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raja_Todar_Mal" title="Raja Todar Mal">Raja Todar Mal</a>. Over the next six years, the Mughals contained the Yusufzai in the mountain valleys, and forced the submission of many chiefs in Swat and Bajaur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards6_68-4"><a href="#cite_note-Richards6-68">[67]</a></sup> Dozens of forts were built and occupied to secure the region. Akbar's response demonstrated his ability to clamp firm military control over the Afghan tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards6_68-5"><a href="#cite_note-Richards6-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite his pact with the Uzbeks, Akbar nurtured a secret hope of reconquering Central Asia from today's Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Markovitz_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Markovitz-69">[68]</a></sup> However, Badakshan and Balkh remained firmly part of the Uzbek dominions. There was only a transient occupation of the two provinces by the Mughals under his grandson, <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a>, in the mid-17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dani_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dani-67">[66]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Akbar's stay in the northern frontiers was highly fruitful. The last of the rebellious Afghan tribes were subdued by 1600.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dani_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dani-67">[66]</a></sup> The Roshaniyya movement was firmly suppressed. The <a href="/wiki/Afridi" title="Afridi">Afridi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orakzai" title="Orakzai">Orakzai</a> tribes, which had risen up under the Roshaniyyas, had been subjugated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dani_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-Dani-67">[66]</a></sup> The leaders of the movement were captured and driven into exile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dani_67-4"><a href="#cite_note-Dani-67">[66]</a></sup> Jalaluddin, the son of the Roshaniyya movement's founder, Bayazid, was killed in 1601 in a fight with Mughal troops near <a href="/wiki/Ghazni" title="Ghazni">Ghazni</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dani_67-5"><a href="#cite_note-Dani-67">[66]</a></sup> Mughal rule over today's Afghanistan was finally secure, particularly after the passing of the Uzbek threat with the death of Abdullah Khan in 1598.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards6_68-6"><a href="#cite_note-Richards6-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conquests_in_the_Indus_Valley">Conquests in the Indus Valley</span></h3>
<p>While in Lahore dealing with the Uzbeks, Akbar had sought to subjugate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_valley" title="Indus valley">Indus valley</a> to secure the frontier provinces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards6_68-7"><a href="#cite_note-Richards6-68">[67]</a></sup> He sent an army to conquer <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a> in the upper Indus basin when, in 1585, Ali Shah, the reigning king of the Shia Chak dynasty, refused to send his son as a hostage to the Mughal court. Ali Shah surrendered immediately to the Mughals, but another of his sons, Yaqub, crowned himself as king, and led a stubborn resistance to Mughal armies. Finally, in June, 1589, Akbar himself travelled from Lahore to Srinagar to receive the surrender of Yaqub and his rebel forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards6_68-8"><a href="#cite_note-Richards6-68">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baltistan" title="Baltistan">Baltistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ladakh" title="Ladakh">Ladakh</a>, which were Tibetan provinces adjacent to Kashmir, pledged their allegiance to Akbar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly6_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly6-70">[69]</a></sup> The Mughals also moved to conquer <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a> in the lower Indus valley. Since 1574, the northern fortress of <a href="/wiki/Bhakkar" title="Bhakkar">Bhakkar</a> had remained under imperial control. Now, in 1586, the Mughal governor of Multan tried and failed to secure the capitulation of Mirza Jani Beg, the independent ruler of <a href="/wiki/Thatta" title="Thatta">Thatta</a> in southern Sindh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards6_68-9"><a href="#cite_note-Richards6-68">[67]</a></sup> Akbar responded by sending a Mughal army to besiege <a href="/wiki/Sehwan" title="Sehwan">Sehwan</a>, the river capital of the region. Jani Beg mustered a large army to meet the Mughals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards6_68-10"><a href="#cite_note-Richards6-68">[67]</a></sup> The outnumbered Mughal forces defeated the Sindhi forces at the Battle of Sehwan. After suffering further defeats, Jani Beg surrendered to the Mughals in 1591, and in 1593, paid homage to Akbar in Lahore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly6_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly6-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subjugation_of_parts_of_Baluchistan">Subjugation of parts of Baluchistan</span></h3>
<p>As early as 1586, about half a dozen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baluch_people" title="Baluch people">Baluchi</a> chiefs, under nominal Pani Afghan rule, had been persuaded to subordinate themselves to Akbar. In preparations to take <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a> from the Safavids, Akbar ordered the Mughal forces to conquer the rest of the Afghan held parts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baluchistan" title="Baluchistan">Baluchistan</a> in 1595.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly6_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly6-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mehta_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mehta-71">[70]</a></sup> The Mughal general, <a href="/wiki/Masum_Shah" title="Masum Shah">Mir Masum</a>, led an attack on the stronghold of Sibi, northeast of <a href="/wiki/Quetta" title="Quetta">Quetta</a> and defeated a coalition of local chieftains in battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mehta_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mehta-71">[70]</a></sup> They were made to acknowledge Mughal supremacy and attend Akbar's court. As a result, the modern-day Pakistani and Afghan parts of Baluchistan, including the <a href="/wiki/Makran" title="Makran">Makran</a> coast, became a part of the Mughal Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mehta_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mehta-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safavids_and_Kandahar">Safavids and Kandahar</span></h3>
<p>Kandahar was the name given by Arab historians to the ancient Indian kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Houtsma_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Houtsma-72">[71]</a></sup> It was intimately connected with the Mughals since the time of their ancestor, <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a>, the warlord who had conquered much of Western, Central, and parts of South Asia in the 14th century. However, the Safavids considered it as an appanage of the Persian ruled territory of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a> and declared its association with the Mughal emperors to be a usurpation. In 1558, while Akbar was consolidating his rule over northern India, the Safavid emperor, <a href="/wiki/Tahmasp_I" title="Tahmasp I">Tahmasp I</a>, had seized Kandahar and expelled its Mughal governor. For the next thirty years, it remained under Persian rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly6_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly6-70">[69]</a></sup> The recovery of Kandahar had not been a priority for Akbar, but after his prolonged military activity in the northern frontiers, a move to restore Mughal rule over the region became desirable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly6_70-4"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly6-70">[69]</a></sup> The conquests of Sindh, Kashmir and parts of Baluchistan, and the ongoing consolidation of Mughal power over today's Afghanistan had added to Akbar's confidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly6_70-5"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly6-70">[69]</a></sup> Furthermore, Kandahar was at this time under threat from the Uzbeks, but the Emperor of Persia, himself beleaguered by the Ottoman Turks, was unable to send any reinforcements. Circumstances favoured the Mughals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly6_70-6"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly6-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1593, Akbar received the exiled Safavid prince, Rostam Mirza, after he had quarrelled with his family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Floor_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Floor-73">[72]</a></sup> Rostam Mirza pledged allegiance to the Mughals; he was granted a rank (mansab) of commander of 5000 men and received Multan as a jagir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Floor_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Floor-73">[72]</a></sup> Beleaguered by constant Uzbek raids, and seeing the reception of Rostom Mirza at the Mughal court, the Safavid prince and governor of Kandahar, Mozaffar Hosayn, also agreed to defect to the Mughals. Mozaffar Hosayn, who was in any case in an adversary relationship with his overlord, <a href="/wiki/Abbas_the_Great" title="Abbas the Great">Shah Abbas</a>, was granted a rank of 5000 men, and his daughter <a href="/wiki/Kandahari_Begum" title="Kandahari Begum">Kandahari Begum</a> was married to Akbar's grandson, the Mughal prince, <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Khurram</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly6_70-7"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly6-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Floor_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Floor-73">[72]</a></sup> Kandahar was finally secured in 1595 with the arrival of a garrison headed by the Mughal general, Shah Bayg Khan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Floor_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-Floor-73">[72]</a></sup> The reconquest of Kandahar did not overtly disturb the Mughal-Persian relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly6_70-8"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly6-70">[69]</a></sup> Akbar and the Persian Shah continued to exchange ambassadors and presents. However, the power equation between the two had now changed in favour of the Mughals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly6_70-9"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly6-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deccan_Sultans">Deccan Sultans</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jalal_al-Din_Muhammad_Akbar._AH_963-1014_AD_1556-1605._AV_Mohur_Falcon_type._Asir_mint._Dated_Khurdad_Ilahi_year_45_(20_February_%E2%80%93_20_March_AD_1600).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="409" data-file-width="817" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Jalal_al-Din_Muhammad_Akbar._AH_963-1014_AD_1556-1605._AV_Mohur_Falcon_type._Asir_mint._Dated_Khurdad_Ilahi_year_45_%2820_February_%E2%80%93_20_March_AD_1600%29.jpg/300px-Jalal_al-Din_Muhammad_Akbar._AH_963-1014_AD_1556-1605._AV_Mohur_Falcon_type._Asir_mint._Dated_Khurdad_Ilahi_year_45_%2820_February_%E2%80%93_20_March_AD_1600%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Jalal_al-Din_Muhammad_Akbar._AH_963-1014_AD_1556-1605._AV_Mohur_Falcon_type._Asir_mint._Dated_Khurdad_Ilahi_year_45_%2820_February_%E2%80%93_20_March_AD_1600%29.jpg/450px-Jalal_al-Din_Muhammad_Akbar._AH_963-1014_AD_1556-1605._AV_Mohur_Falcon_type._Asir_mint._Dated_Khurdad_Ilahi_year_45_%2820_February_%E2%80%93_20_March_AD_1600%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Jalal_al-Din_Muhammad_Akbar._AH_963-1014_AD_1556-1605._AV_Mohur_Falcon_type._Asir_mint._Dated_Khurdad_Ilahi_year_45_%2820_February_%E2%80%93_20_March_AD_1600%29.jpg/600px-Jalal_al-Din_Muhammad_Akbar._AH_963-1014_AD_1556-1605._AV_Mohur_Falcon_type._Asir_mint._Dated_Khurdad_Ilahi_year_45_%2820_February_%E2%80%93_20_March_AD_1600%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jalal_al-Din_Muhammad_Akbar._AH_963-1014_AD_1556-1605._AV_Mohur_Falcon_type._Asir_mint._Dated_Khurdad_Ilahi_year_45_(20_February_%E2%80%93_20_March_AD_1600).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Falcon <a href="/wiki/Mohur" title="Mohur">Mohur</a> of Akbar, minted in Asir. This coin was issued in the name of Akbar, to commemorate the capture of the strategic <a href="/wiki/Asirgarh_Fort" title="Asirgarh Fort">Asirgarh Fort</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khandesh_Sultanate" title="Khandesh Sultanate">Khandesh Sultanate</a> on 17 January 1601 CE. Legend: <i>"Allah is great, <a href="/wiki/Khordad" title="Khordad">Khordad</a> Ilahi 45, struck at Asir"</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1917274_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1917274-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deccan_sultanates" title="Deccan sultanates">Deccan sultanates</a></div>
<p>In 1593, Akbar began military operations against the Deccan Sultans who had not submitted to his authority. He besieged <a href="/wiki/Ahmednagar_Fort" title="Ahmednagar Fort">Ahmednagar Fort</a> in 1595, forcing <a href="/wiki/Chand_Bibi" title="Chand Bibi">Chand Bibi</a> to cede <a href="/wiki/Berar_Subah" title="Berar Subah">Berar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> A subsequent revolt forced Akbar to take the fort in August 1600. Akbar occupied <a href="/wiki/Burhanpur" title="Burhanpur">Burhanpur</a> and besieged <a href="/wiki/Asirgarh_Fort" title="Asirgarh Fort">Asirgarh Fort</a> in 1599, and took it on 17 January 1601, when Miran Bahadur Shah refused to submit <a href="/wiki/Khandesh" title="Khandesh">Khandesh</a>. Akbar then established the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subah_(province)" title="Subah (province)">Subahs</a> of Ahmadnagar, Berar and Khandesh under Prince Daniyal. "By the time of his death in 1605, Akbar controlled a broad sweep of territory from the Bay of Bengal to Qandahar and Badakshan. He touched the western sea in Sind and at <a href="/wiki/Surat" title="Surat">Surat</a> and was well astride central India."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen2_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-sen2-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration">Administration</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_government">Political government</span></h3>
<p>Akbar's system of central government was based on the system that had evolved since the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a>, but the functions of various departments were carefully reorganised by laying down detailed regulations for their functioning<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The revenue department was headed by a <i>wazir</i>, responsible for all finances and management of <i>jagir</i> and <i>inam</i> lands.</li>
<li>The head of the military was called the <i>mir bakshi</i>, appointed from among the leading nobles of the court. The <i>mir bakshi</i> was in charge of intelligence gathering, and also made recommendations to the emperor for military appointments and promotions.</li>
<li>The <i>mir saman</i> was in charge of the imperial household, including the harems, and supervised the functioning of the court and royal bodyguard.</li>
<li>The judiciary was a separate organisation headed by a chief <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qazi" title="Qazi">qazi</a></i>, who was also responsible for religious beliefs and practices</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxation">Taxation</span></h3>
<p>Akbar set about reforming the administration of his empire's land revenue by adopting a system that had been used by <a href="/wiki/Sher_Shah_Suri" title="Sher Shah Suri">Sher Shah Suri</a>. A cultivated area where crops grew well was measured and taxed through fixed rates based on the area's crop and productivity. However, this placed hardship on the peasantry because tax rates were fixed on the basis of prices prevailing in the imperial court, which were often higher than those in the countryside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> Akbar changed to a decentralised system of annual assessment, but this resulted in corruption among local officials and was abandoned in 1580, to be replaced by a system called the <i><span title="Bengali-language romanization"><i lang="bn-Latn">dahsala</i></span></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dahsala_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-dahsala-79">[78]</a></sup> Under the new system, revenue was calculated as one-third of the average produce of the previous ten years, to be paid to the state in cash. This system was later refined, taking into account local prices, and grouping areas with similar productivity into assessment circles. Remission was given to peasants when the harvest failed during times of flood or drought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dahsala_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-dahsala-79">[78]</a></sup> Akbar's <i><span title="Bengali-language romanization"><i lang="bn-Latn">dahsala</i></span></i> system (also known as <i><span title="Bengali-language romanization"><i lang="bn-Latn">zabti</i></span>)</i> is credited to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raja_Todar_Mal" title="Raja Todar Mal">Raja Todar Mal</a>, who also served as a revenue officer under Sher Shah Suri,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra_2007_236_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra_2007_236-80">[79]</a></sup> and the structure of the revenue administration was set out by the latter in a detailed memorandum submitted to the emperor in 1582â83.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other local methods of assessment continued in some areas. Land which was fallow or uncultivated was charged at concessional rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-localassessment_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-localassessment-82">[81]</a></sup> Akbar also actively encouraged the improvement and extension of agriculture. The village continued to remain the primary unit of revenue assessment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zamindar" title="Zamindar">Zamindars</a> of every area were required to provide loans and agricultural implements in times of need, to encourage farmers to plough as much land as possible and to sow seeds of superior quality. In turn, the zamindars were given a hereditary right to collect a share of the produce. Peasants had a hereditary right to cultivate the land as long as they paid the land revenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-localassessment_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-localassessment-82">[81]</a></sup> While the revenue assessment system showed concern for the small peasantry, it also maintained a level of distrust towards the revenue officials. Revenue officials were guaranteed only three-quarters of their salary, with the remaining quarter dependent on their full realisation of the revenue assessed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_organization">Military organization</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mansabdari" title="Mansabdari">Mansabdari</a></div>
<p>Akbar organised his army as well as the nobility by means of a system called the <i>mansabdari</i>. Under this system, each officer in the army was assigned a rank (a <i>mansabdar</i>), and assigned a number of <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a> that he had to supply to the imperial army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra_2007_236_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra_2007_236-80">[79]</a></sup> The <i>mansabdars</i> were divided into 33 classes. The top three commanding ranks, ranging from 7,000 to 10,000 troops, were normally reserved for princes. Other ranks between 10 and 5,000 were assigned to other members of the nobility. The empire's permanent <a href="/wiki/Standing_army" title="Standing army">standing army</a> was quite small and the imperial forces mostly consisted of contingents maintained by the <i>mansabdars</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Persons were normally appointed to a low <i>mansab</i> and then promoted, based on their merit as well as the favour of the emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mansabdari_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-mansabdari-86">[85]</a></sup> Each <i>mansabdar</i> was required to maintain a certain number of cavalrymen and twice that number of horses. The number of horses was greater because they had to be rested and rapidly replaced in times of war. Akbar employed strict measures to ensure that the quality of the armed forces was maintained at a high level; horses were regularly inspected and only <a href="/wiki/Arabian_horse" title="Arabian horse">Arabian horses</a> were normally employed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> The <i>mansabdars</i> were remunerated well for their services and constituted the highest paid military service in the world at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mansabdari_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-mansabdari-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capital">Capital</span></h3>
