<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Audienzhalle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Audienzhalle.jpg/220px-Audienzhalle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Audienzhalle.jpg/330px-Audienzhalle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Audienzhalle.jpg/440px-Audienzhalle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Audienzhalle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Diwan-i-Khas</i> (Hall of Private Audience) in Fatehpur Sikri</div></div></div>
<p>Akbar was a follower of <a href="/wiki/Salim_Chishti" title="Salim Chishti">Salim Chishti</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">holy man</a> who lived in the region of Sikri near Agra. Believing the area to be a lucky one for himself, he had a mosque constructed there for the use of the priest. Subsequently, he celebrated the victories over Chittor and Ranthambore by laying the foundation of a new walled capital, 23 miles (37Â km) west of Agra in 1569, which was named Fatehpur ("<i>town of victory</i>") after the conquest of Gujarat in 1573 and subsequently came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Fatehpur_Sikri" title="Fatehpur Sikri">Fatehpur Sikri</a> in order to distinguish it from other similarly named towns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earlyconquest_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-earlyconquest-54">[53]</a></sup> Palaces for each of Akbar's senior queens, a huge artificial lake, and sumptuous water-filled courtyards were built there. However, the city was soon abandoned and the capital was moved to <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a> in 1585. The reason may have been that the water supply in Fatehpur Sikri was insufficient or of poor quality. Or, as some historians believe, Akbar had to attend to the northwest areas of his empire and therefore moved his capital northwest. Other sources indicate Akbar simply lost interest in the city<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> or realised it was not militarily defensible. In 1599, Akbar shifted his capital back to Agra from where he reigned until his death.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade">Trade</span></h3>
<p>The reign of Akbar was characterized by commercial expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Economy_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Economy-89">[88]</a></sup> The Mughal government encouraged traders, provided protection and security for transactions and levied a very low custom duty to stimulate foreign trade. Furthermore, it strived to foster a climate conducive to commerce by requiring local administrators to provide restitution to traders for goods stolen while in their territory. To minimize such incidents, bands of highway police called <i><span title="Bengali-language romanization"><i lang="bn-Latn">rahdars</i></span></i> were enlisted to patrol roads and ensure the safety of traders. Other active measures taken included the construction and protection of routes of commerce and communications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levi_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levi-90">[89]</a></sup> Indeed, Akbar would make concerted efforts to improve roads to facilitate the use of wheeled vehicles through the <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pass" title="Khyber Pass">Khyber Pass</a>, the most popular route frequented by traders and travelers journeying from <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a> into Mughal India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levi_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Levi-90">[89]</a></sup> He also strategically occupied the northwestern cities of <a href="/wiki/Multan" title="Multan">Multan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a> and constructed great forts, such as the one at <a href="/wiki/Attock_Fort" title="Attock Fort">Attock</a> near the crossing of the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Trunk_Road" title="Grand Trunk Road">Grand Trunk Road</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_river" title="Indus river">Indus river</a>, as well as a network of smaller forts called <i>thanas</i> throughout the frontier to secure the overland trade with Persia and Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levi_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-Levi-90">[89]</a></sup> Furthermore, he established a trade business for his chief consort, <a href="/wiki/Mariam-uz-Zamani" title="Mariam-uz-Zamani">Mariam-uz-Zamani</a> who ran an extensive trade of indigo, spices, and cotton to Gulf nations through merchant's vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DirkCollier_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-DirkCollier-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coins">Coins</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="588" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg/220px-Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg/330px-Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg/440px-Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver coin of Akbar with inscriptions of the <a href="/wiki/Shahada" title="Shahada">Islamic declaration of faith</a>, the declaration reads: "There is no god except Allah, and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> is the messenger of Allah."</div></div></div>
<p>Akbar was a great innovator as far as coinage is concerned. The coins of Akbar set a new chapter in India's numismatic history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> The coins of Akbar's grandfather, Babur, and father, Humayun, are basic and devoid of any innovation as the former was busy establishing the foundations of the Mughal rule in India while the latter was ousted by the Afghan, Sher Shah Suri, and returned to the throne only to die a year later. While the reign of both Babur and Humayun represented turmoil, Akbar's relative long reign of 50 years allowed him to experiment with coinage.
</p><p>Akbar introduced coins with decorative floral motifs, dotted borders, quatrefoil and other types. His coins were both round and square in shape with a unique 'mehrab' (lozenge) shape coin highlighting numismatic calligraphy at its best.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> Akbar's portrait type gold coin (Mohur) is generally attributed to his son, Prince Salim (later Emperor Jahangir), who had rebelled and then sought reconciliation thereafter by minting and presenting his father with gold Mohur's bearing Akbar's portrait. The tolerant view of Akbar is represented by the 'Ram-Sita' silver coin type while during the latter part of Akbar's reign, we see coins portraying the concept of Akbar's newly promoted religion 'Din-e-ilahi' with the Ilahi type and Jalla Jalal-Hu type coins.
</p><p>The coins,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> left, represent examples of these innovative concepts introduced by Akbar that set the precedent for Mughal coins which was refined and perfected by his son, Jahangir, and later by his grandson, Shah Jahan.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diplomacy">Diplomacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Matrimonial_alliances">Matrimonial alliances</span></h3>
<p>The practice of arranging marriages between Hindu princesses and Muslim kings was known much before Akbar's time, but in most cases, these marriages did not lead to any stable relations between the families involved, and the women were lost to their families and did not return after marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly-94">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra_243_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra_243-95">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar_37_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar_37-96">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, Akbar's policy of matrimonial alliances marked a departure in India from previous practice in that the marriage itself marked the beginning of a new order of relations, wherein the Hindu Rajputs who married their daughters or sisters to him would be treated on par with his Muslim fathers-in-law and brothers-in-law in all respects except being able to dine and pray with him or take Muslim wives. These Rajputs were made members of his court and their daughters' or sisters' marriage to a Muslim ceased to be a sign of degradation, except for certain proud elements who still considered it a sign of humiliation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar_37_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar_37-96">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Birth_of_jahangir.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="532" decoding="async" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Birth_of_jahangir.jpg/220px-Birth_of_jahangir.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Birth_of_jahangir.jpg/330px-Birth_of_jahangir.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Birth_of_jahangir.jpg/440px-Birth_of_jahangir.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Birth_of_jahangir.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Mariam-uz-Zamani" title="Mariam-uz-Zamani">Empress Mariam-uz-Zamani</a>, commonly known as Jodha Bai, giving birth to Prince Salim, the future emperor Jahangir.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kacchwaha" title="Kacchwaha">Kacchwaha</a> Rajput, Raja <a href="/wiki/Bharmal" title="Bharmal">Bharmal</a>, of the small kingdom of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amer,_India" title="Amer, India">Amer</a>, who had come to Akbar's court shortly after the latter's accession, allied by giving his daughter <a href="/wiki/Mariam-uz-Zamani" title="Mariam-uz-Zamani">Harka Bai</a>, mother of Akbar's successor, in marriage to the emperor. Bharmal was made a noble of high rank in the imperial court, and subsequently, his son <a href="/wiki/Bhagwant_Das" title="Bhagwant Das">Bhagwant Das</a> and grandson <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man_Singh" title="Man Singh">Man Singh</a> also rose to high ranks in the nobility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra_243_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra_243-95">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other Rajput kingdoms also established matrimonial alliances with Akbar, but matrimony was not insisted on as a precondition for forming alliances. Two major Rajput clans remained aloof â the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sisodiya" title="Sisodiya">Sisodiyas</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mewar" title="Mewar">Mewar</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hada_(clan)" title="Hada (clan)">Hadas</a> of Ranthambore. In another turning point of Akbar's reign, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raja_Man_Singh" title="Raja Man Singh">Raja Man Singh</a> I of Amber went with Akbar to meet the Hada leader, Surjan Hada, to effect an alliance. Surjan accepted an alliance on the condition that Akbar did not marry any of his daughters. Consequently, no matrimonial alliance was entered into, yet Surjan was made a noble and placed in charge of Garh-Katanga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra_243_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra_243-95">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>The political effect of these alliances was significant. While some Rajput women who entered Akbar's harem converted to Islam, they were generally provided full religious freedom, and their relatives, who continued to remain Hindu, formed a significant part of the nobility and served to articulate the opinions of the majority of the common populace in the imperial court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra_243_95-3"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra_243-95">[94]</a></sup> The interaction between Hindu and Muslim nobles in the imperial court resulted in an exchange of thoughts and blending of the two cultures. Further, newer generations of the Mughal line represented a merger of Mughal and Rajput blood, thereby strengthening ties between the two. As a result, the Rajputs became the strongest allies of the Mughals, and Rajput soldiers and generals fought for the Mughal army under Akbar, leading it in several campaigns including the conquest of Gujarat in 1572.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> Akbar's policy of religious tolerance ensured that employment in the imperial administration was open to all on merit irrespective of creed, and this led to an increase in the strength of the administrative services of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another legend is that Akbar's daughter Meherunnissa was enamored by <a href="/wiki/Tansen" title="Tansen">Tansen</a> and had a role in his coming to Akbar's court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dawn_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-dawn-99">[98]</a></sup> Tansen converted to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> from <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, apparently on the eve of his marriage with Akbar's daughter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_the_Portuguese">Relations with the Portuguese</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" â ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" â ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25%; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p><i>An Emperor shall be ever Intent on Conquest, Otherwise, His enemies shall rise in arms against him.</i>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""><b>Jalal-ud-Din Muhammad Akbar</b></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>At the time of Akbar's ascension in 1556, the Portuguese had established several fortresses and factories on the western coast of the subcontinent, and largely controlled navigation and sea trade in that region. As a consequence of this colonialism, all other trading entities were subject to the terms and conditions of the Portuguese, and this was resented by the rulers and traders of the time including <a href="/wiki/Bahadur_Shah_of_Gujarat" title="Bahadur Shah of Gujarat">Bahadur Shah of Gujarat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Death_of_Sultan_Bahadur_in_front_of_Diu_against_the_Portuguese_1537_Akbar_Nama_end_of_16th_century.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2877" data-file-width="1759" decoding="async" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Death_of_Sultan_Bahadur_in_front_of_Diu_against_the_Portuguese_1537_Akbar_Nama_end_of_16th_century.jpg/170px-Death_of_Sultan_Bahadur_in_front_of_Diu_against_the_Portuguese_1537_Akbar_Nama_end_of_16th_century.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Death_of_Sultan_Bahadur_in_front_of_Diu_against_the_Portuguese_1537_Akbar_Nama_end_of_16th_century.jpg/255px-Death_of_Sultan_Bahadur_in_front_of_Diu_against_the_Portuguese_1537_Akbar_Nama_end_of_16th_century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Death_of_Sultan_Bahadur_in_front_of_Diu_against_the_Portuguese_1537_Akbar_Nama_end_of_16th_century.jpg/340px-Death_of_Sultan_Bahadur_in_front_of_Diu_against_the_Portuguese_1537_Akbar_Nama_end_of_16th_century.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Death_of_Sultan_Bahadur_in_front_of_Diu_against_the_Portuguese_1537_Akbar_Nama_end_of_16th_century.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Death of <a href="/wiki/Bahadur_Shah_of_Gujarat" title="Bahadur Shah of Gujarat">Bahadur Shah of Gujarat</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Diu" title="Siege of Diu">Diu</a>, in front of the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a> in 1537<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In the year 1572 the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> annexed <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a> and acquired its first access to the sea after local officials informed Akbar that the Portuguese had begun to exert control in the Indian Ocean. Hence Akbar was conscious of the threat posed by the presence of the Portuguese and remained content with obtaining a <i><a href="/wiki/Cartaz" title="Cartaz">cartaz</a></i> (permit) from them for sailing in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> At the initial meeting of the Mughals and the Portuguese during the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Siege_of_Surat&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Siege of Surat (page does not exist)">Siege of Surat</a> in 1572, the Portuguese, recognizing the superior strength of the Mughal army, chose to adopt diplomacy instead of war. The Portuguese Governor, upon the request of Akbar, sent him an ambassador to establish friendly relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> Akbar's efforts to purchase and secure from the Portuguese some of their compact <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> pieces were unsuccessful and thus Akbar could not establish the Mughal navy along the Gujarat coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Akbar accepted the offer of diplomacy, but the Portuguese continually asserted their authority and power in the Indian Ocean; Akbar was highly concerned when he had to request a permit from the Portuguese before any ships from the Mughal Empire were to depart for the <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a> pilgrimage to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> In 1573, he issued a <i><a href="/wiki/Firman" title="Firman">firman</a></i> directing Mughal administrative officials in Gujarat not to provoke the Portuguese in the territory they held in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daman_District,_India" title="Daman District, India">Daman</a>. The Portuguese, in turn, issued passes for the members of Akbar's family to go on Hajj to Mecca. The Portuguese made mention of the extraordinary status of the vessel and the special status to be accorded to its occupants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> Furthermore, he established a trade business for his favourite consort, <a href="/wiki/Mariam-uz-Zamani" title="Mariam-uz-Zamani">Mariam-uz-Zamani</a> who ran an extensive trade of indigo, spices, and cotton to the Gulf nations through merchant's vessels. The cost of her largest ship named 'Rahimi' built on the orders of Akbar is estimated to be around 300000 pounds (Rs 3 crores approximately).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DirkCollier_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-DirkCollier-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 1579 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuits</a> from <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">Goa</a> were invited to visit the court of Akbar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> The emperor had his scribes translate the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> and granted the Jesuits freedom to preach the Gospel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-org_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-org-110">[109]</a></sup> One of his sons, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultan_Murad_Mirza" title="Sultan Murad Mirza">Sultan Murad Mirza</a>, was entrusted to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Antoni_de_Montserrat&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Antoni de Montserrat (page does not exist)">Antoni de Montserrat</a> for his education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durant2011_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Durant2011-112">[111]</a></sup> While debating at court, the Jesuits did not confine themselves to the exposition of their own beliefs but also reviled Islam and Muhammad. Their comments enraged the <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">Imams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">Ulama</a>, who objected to the remarks, but Akbar ordered their comments to be recorded and observed the Jesuits and their behavior. This event was followed by a rebellion of Muslim clerics in 1581 led by Mullah Muhammad Yazdi and Muiz-ul-Mulk, the chief <a href="/wiki/Qadi" title="Qadi">Qadi</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a>; the rebels wanted to overthrow Akbar and insert his brother Mirza Muhammad Hakim ruler of Kabul on the Mughal throne. Akbar successfully defeated the rebels, but he had grown more cautious about his guests and his proclamations, which he later checked with his advisers carefully.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_the_Ottoman_Empire">Relations with the Ottoman Empire</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="509" data-file-width="507" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png/220px-Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png/330px-Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png/440px-Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a> ambush against the galleys of <a href="/wiki/Seydi_Ali_Reis" title="Seydi Ali Reis">Seydi Ali Reis</a> (Akbar's allies) in the Indian Ocean.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1555, while Akbar was still a child, the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> Admiral <a href="/wiki/Seydi_Ali_Reis" title="Seydi Ali Reis">Seydi Ali Reis</a> visited the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mughal_Emperor" title="Mughal Emperor">Mughal Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a>. In 1569, during the early years of Akbar's rule, another Ottoman Admiral <a href="/wiki/Kurto%C4%9Flu_H%C4%B1z%C4%B1r_Reis" title="KurtoÄlu HÄ±zÄ±r Reis">KurtoÄlu HÄ±zÄ±r Reis</a> arrived on the shores of the Mughal Empire. These Ottoman admirals sought to end the growing threats of the Portuguese Empire during their <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_naval_expeditions_in_the_Indian_Ocean" title="Ottoman naval expeditions in the Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean campaigns</a>. During his reign Akbar himself is known to have sent six documents addressing the Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultan</a> <a href="/wiki/Suleiman_the_Magnificent" title="Suleiman the Magnificent">Suleiman the Magnificent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1576 Akbar sent a very large contingent of pilgrims led by Khwaja Sultan Naqshbandi, <a href="/wiki/Yahya_Saleh" title="Yahya Saleh">Yahya Saleh</a>, with 600,000 gold and silver coins and 12,000 <a href="/wiki/Kaftan" title="Kaftan">Kaftans</a> of honour and large consignments of rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2016)">pageÂ needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In October 1576 Akbar sent a delegation including members of his family, including his aunt Gulbadan Begum and his consort Salima, on Hajj by two ships from <a href="/wiki/Surat" title="Surat">Surat</a> including an Ottoman vessel, which reached the port of <a href="/wiki/Jeddah" title="Jeddah">Jeddah</a> in 1577 and then proceeded towards <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> Four more caravans were sent from 1577 to 1580, with exquisite gifts for the authorities of Mecca and Medina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>The imperial Mughal entourage stayed in Mecca and Medina for nearly four years and attended the <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a> four times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> During this period Akbar financed the pilgrimages of many poor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> from the Mughal Empire and also funded the foundations of the <a href="/wiki/Qadiriyya" title="Qadiriyya">Qadiriyya</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> Order's dervish lodge in the Hijaz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faroqhi2006_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faroqhi2006-121">[120]</a></sup> The Mughals eventually set out for Surat, and their return was assisted by the Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Pasha" title="Pasha">Pasha</a> in Jeddah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> Because of Akbar's attempts to build Mughal presence in Mecca and Medina, the local Sharif's began to have more confidence in the financial support provided by Mughal Empire, lessening their dependency upon Ottoman bounty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faroqhi2006_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Faroqhi2006-121">[120]</a></sup> Mughal-Ottoman trade also flourished during this period â in fact merchants loyal to Akbar are known to have reached <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a> after journeying upriver through the port of <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to some accounts Akbar expressed a desire to form an alliance with the Portuguese, mainly in order to advance his interests, but whenever the Portuguese attempted to invade the Ottomans, Akbar proved abortive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> In 1587 a Portuguese fleet sent to attack Yemen was ferociously routed and defeated by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Navy" title="Ottoman Navy">Ottoman Navy</a>; thereafter the Mughal-Portuguese alliance immediately collapsed, mainly because of the continuing pressure by the Mughal Empire's prestigious vassals at <a href="/wiki/Janjira_State" title="Janjira State">Janjira</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_the_Safavid_dynasty">Relations with the Safavid dynasty</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AkbariMosqueOverlookingGanges-Sita-Ram1804.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2619" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/AkbariMosqueOverlookingGanges-Sita-Ram1804.jpg/220px-AkbariMosqueOverlookingGanges-Sita-Ram1804.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/AkbariMosqueOverlookingGanges-Sita-Ram1804.jpg/330px-AkbariMosqueOverlookingGanges-Sita-Ram1804.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/AkbariMosqueOverlookingGanges-Sita-Ram1804.jpg/440px-AkbariMosqueOverlookingGanges-Sita-Ram1804.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AkbariMosqueOverlookingGanges-Sita-Ram1804.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Akbari Mosque, overlooking the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safavid_Empire" title="Safavid Empire">Safavids</a> and the Mughals had a long history of diplomatic relationship, with the Safavid ruler <a href="/wiki/Tahmasp_I" title="Tahmasp I">Tahmasp I</a> having provided refuge to <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a> when he had to flee the Indian subcontinent following his defeat by Sher Shah Suri. However, the Safavids differed from the Sunni Mughals and Ottomans in following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shiite" title="Shiite">Shiite</a> sect of Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup> One of the longest standing disputes between the Safavids and the Mughals pertained to the control of the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qandahar" title="Qandahar">Qandahar</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindukush" title="Hindukush">Hindukush</a> region, forming the border between the two empires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Majumdar_153_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Majumdar_153-128">[127]</a></sup> The Hindukush region was militarily very significant owing to its geography, and this was well-recognised by strategists of the times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> Consequently, the city, which was being administered by Bairam Khan at the time of Akbar's accession, was invaded and captured by the Persian ruler Husain Mirza, a <a href="/wiki/Ismail_I#Offspring" title="Ismail I">cousin of Tahmasp I</a>, in 1558.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Majumdar_153_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Majumdar_153-128">[127]</a></sup> Subsequent to this, Bairam Khan sent an envoy to the court of Tahmasp I in an effort to maintain peaceful relations with the Safavids. This gesture was reciprocated and a cordial relationship continued to prevail between the two empires during the first two decades of Akbar's reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> However, the death of Tahmasp I in 1576 resulted in civil war and instability in the Safavid empire, and diplomatic relations between the two empires ceased for more than a decade. They were restored only in 1587 following the accession of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbas_I_of_Persia" title="Abbas I of Persia">Shah Abbas</a> to the Safavid throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> Shortly afterwards, Akbar's army completed its annexation of Kabul, and in order to further secure the north-western boundaries of his empire, it proceeded to Qandahar. The city capitulated without resistance on 18 April 1595, and the ruler Muzaffar Hussain moved into Akbar's court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> Qandahar continued to remain in Mughal possession, and the Hindukush the empire's western frontier, for several decades until <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a>'s expedition into <a href="/wiki/Badakhshan" title="Badakhshan">Badakhshan</a> in 1646.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> Diplomatic relations continued to be maintained between the Safavid and Mughal courts until the end of Akbar's reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_other_contemporary_kingdoms">Relations with other contemporary kingdoms</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vincent_Arthur_Smith" title="Vincent Arthur Smith">Vincent Arthur Smith</a> observes that the merchant Mildenhall was employed in 1600 while the establishment of the company was under adjustment to bear a letter from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_I" title="Queen Elizabeth I">Queen Elizabeth</a> to Akbar requesting liberty to trade in his dominions on terms as good as those enjoyed by the Portuguese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>Akbar was also visited by the French explorer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pierre_Malherbe" title="Pierre Malherbe">Pierre Malherbe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_policy">Religious policy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Emperor_Akbar_Praying.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1374" data-file-width="810" decoding="async" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Portrait_of_Emperor_Akbar_Praying.jpg/170px-Portrait_of_Emperor_Akbar_Praying.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Portrait_of_Emperor_Akbar_Praying.jpg/255px-Portrait_of_Emperor_Akbar_Praying.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Portrait_of_Emperor_Akbar_Praying.jpg/340px-Portrait_of_Emperor_Akbar_Praying.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Emperor_Akbar_Praying.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of the Mughal Emperor Akbar invocation of a <a href="/wiki/Dua" title="Dua">Dua</a> prayer.</div></div></div>
<p>Akbar, as well as his mother and other members of his family, are believed to have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup> His early days were spent in the backdrop of an atmosphere in which liberal sentiments were encouraged and <a href="/wiki/Fundamentalism" title="Fundamentalism">religious narrow-mindedness</a> was frowned upon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion1_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-religion1-138">[137]</a></sup> From the 15th century, a number of rulers in various parts of the country adopted a more liberal policy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_toleration" title="Religious toleration">religious tolerance</a>, attempting to foster <a href="/wiki/Communalism_(South_Asia)" title="Communalism (South Asia)">communal harmony</a> between Hindus and Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion2_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-religion2-139">[138]</a></sup> These sentiments were earlier encouraged by the teachings of popular saints like <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kabir" title="Kabir">Kabir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chaitanya_Mahaprabhu" title="Chaitanya Mahaprabhu">Chaitanya</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion1_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-religion1-138">[137]</a></sup> the verses of the Persian poet <a href="/wiki/Hafez" title="Hafez">Hafez</a> which advocated human sympathy and a liberal outlook,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup> as well as the Timurid ethos of religious tolerance in the empire, persisted in the polity right from the times of <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> to <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a>, and influenced Akbar's policy of tolerance in matters of religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion3_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-religion3-141">[140]</a></sup> Further, his childhood tutors, who included two Irani Shias, were largely above <a href="/wiki/Sectarianism" title="Sectarianism">sectarian</a> prejudices, and made a significant contribution to Akbar's later inclination towards religious tolerance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion3_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-religion3-141">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>Akbar sponsored religious debates between different Muslim groups (<a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma'ilism">Ismaili</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufis</a>), <a href="/wiki/Parsis" title="Parsis">Parsis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hindus" title="Hindus">Hindus</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaivite" title="Shaivite">Shaivite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnava</a>), <a href="/wiki/Sikhs" title="Sikhs">Sikhs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jesuits" title="Jesuits">Jesuits</a> and <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">Materialists</a>, but was partial to Sufism, he proclaimed that 'the wisdom of Vedanta is the wisdom of Sufism'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>When he was at Fatehpur Sikri, he held discussions as he loved to know about others' religious beliefs. On one such day he got to know that the religious people of other religions were often intolerant of others religious beliefs. This led him to form the idea of the new religion, Sulh-e-kul meaning universal peace. His idea of this religion did not discriminate other religions and focused on the ideas of peace, unity and tolerance.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Association_with_the_Muslim_aristocracy">Association with the Muslim aristocracy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1573-Akbar_receiving_his_sons_at_Fathpur-Akbarnama.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="1678" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/1573-Akbar_receiving_his_sons_at_Fathpur-Akbarnama.jpg/170px-1573-Akbar_receiving_his_sons_at_Fathpur-Akbarnama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/1573-Akbar_receiving_his_sons_at_Fathpur-Akbarnama.jpg/255px-1573-Akbar_receiving_his_sons_at_Fathpur-Akbarnama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/1573-Akbar_receiving_his_sons_at_Fathpur-Akbarnama.jpg/340px-1573-Akbar_receiving_his_sons_at_Fathpur-Akbarnama.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1573-Akbar_receiving_his_sons_at_Fathpur-Akbarnama.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mughal_Emperor" title="Mughal Emperor">Mughal Emperor</a> Akbar welcomes his son <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Prince Salim</a> at Fatehpur Sikri, (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akbarnameh" title="Akbarnameh">Akbarnameh</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>During the early part of his reign, Akbar adopted an attitude of suppression towards Muslim sects that were condemned by the orthodoxy as <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heretical</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion4_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-religion4-143">[142]</a></sup> In 1567, on the advice of Shaikh Abdu'n Nabi, he ordered the exhumation of Mir Murtaza Sharifi Shirazi â a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia" title="Shia">Shia</a> buried in Delhi â because of the grave's proximity to that of <a href="/wiki/Amir_Khusrau" title="Amir Khusrau">Amir Khusrau</a>, arguing that a "heretic" could not be buried so close to the grave of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> saint, reflecting a restrictive attitude towards the Shia, which continued to persist until the early 1570s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion5_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-religion5-144">[143]</a></sup> He suppressed <a href="/wiki/Mahdavia" title="Mahdavia">Mahdavism</a> in 1573 during his campaign in Gujarat, in the course of which the Mahdavi leader Bandagi Miyan Sheik Mustafa was arrested and brought in chains to the court for debate and released after eighteen months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion5_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-religion5-144">[143]</a></sup> However, as Akbar increasingly came under the influence of pantheistic Sufi mysticism from the early 1570s, it caused a great shift in his outlook and culminated in his shift from orthodox Islam as traditionally professed, in favour of a new concept of Islam transcending the limits of religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion5_144-2"><a href="#cite_note-religion5-144">[143]</a></sup> Consequently, during the latter half of his reign, he adopted a policy of tolerance towards the Shias and declared a prohibition on Shia-Sunni conflict, and the empire remained neutral in matters of internal sectarian conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup> In the year 1578, the Mughal Emperor Akbar famously referred to himself as:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Emperor of Islam, Emir of the Faithful, Shadow of God on earth, Abul Fath Jalal-ud-din Muhammad Akbar Badshah Ghazi (whose empire Allah perpetuate), is a most just, most wise, and a most God-fearing ruler.</p></blockquote>
<p>In 1580, a rebellion broke out in the eastern part of Akbar's empire, and a number of <i><a href="/wiki/Fatwa" title="Fatwa">fatwas</a></i>, declaring Akbar to be a heretic, were issued by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qazi" title="Qazi">Qazis</a>. Akbar suppressed the rebellion and handed out severe punishments to the Qazis. To further strengthen his position in dealing with the Qazis, Akbar issued a <i><a href="/wiki/Mazhar" title="Mazhar">mazhar</a></i>, or declaration, that was signed by all major <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulema" title="Ulema">ulemas</a></i> in 1579.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion6_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-religion6-146">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup> The <i>mahzar</i> asserted that Akbar was the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">Khalifa</a></i> of the age, a higher rank than that of a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mujtahid" title="Mujtahid">Mujtahid</a></i>: in case of a difference of opinion among the Mujtahids, Akbar could select any one opinion and could also issue decrees that did not go against the <i><a href="/wiki/Nass_(Islam)" title="Nass (Islam)">nass</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup> Given the prevailing Islamic sectarian conflicts in various parts of the country at that time, it is believed that the <i>Mazhar</i> helped stabilize the religious situation in the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion6_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-religion6-146">[145]</a></sup> It made Akbar very powerful because of the complete supremacy accorded to the <i>Khalifa</i> by Islam, and also helped him eliminate the religious and political influence of the Ottoman <i>Khalifa</i> over his subjects, thus ensuring their complete loyalty to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout his reign Akbar was a patron of influential Muslim scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Mir_Ahmed_Nasrallah_Thattvi" title="Mir Ahmed Nasrallah Thattvi">Mir Ahmed Nasrallah Thattvi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tahir_Muhammad_Thattvi" title="Tahir Muhammad Thattvi">Tahir Muhammad Thattvi</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Whenever Akbar would attend congregations at a mosque the following proclamation was made:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Lord to me the Kingdom gave, He made me wise, strong and brave, He guides me through right and truth, Filling my mind with the love of truth, No praise of man could sum his state, Allah Hu Akbar, God is Great.</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Din-i-Ilahi">Din-i-Ilahi</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Din-i-Ilahi" title="Din-i-Ilahi">Din-i-Ilahi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4123" data-file-width="2275" decoding="async" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg/170px-Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg/255px-Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg/340px-Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Akbar holds a religious assembly of different faiths in the <a href="/wiki/Ibadat_Khana" title="Ibadat Khana">Ibadat Khana</a> in Fatehpur Sikri.</div></div></div>
<p>Akbar was deeply interested in religious and philosophical matters. An orthodox Muslim at the outset, he later came to be influenced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> mysticism that was being preached in the country at that time, and moved away from orthodoxy, appointing to his court several talented people with liberal ideas, including Abul Fazl, <a href="/wiki/Faizi" title="Faizi">Faizi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Birbal" title="Birbal">Birbal</a>. In 1575, he built a hall called the <a href="/wiki/Ibadat_Khana" title="Ibadat Khana">Ibadat Khana</a> (<i>"House of Worship"</i>) at Fatehpur Sikri, to which he invited theologians, mystics and selected courtiers renowned for their intellectual achievements and discussed matters of <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spirituality</a> with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion1_138-2"><a href="#cite_note-religion1-138">[137]</a></sup> These discussions, initially restricted to Muslims, were acrimonious and resulted in the participants shouting at and abusing each other. Upset by this, Akbar opened the Ibadat Khana to people of all religions as well as atheists, resulting in the scope of the discussions broadening and extending even into areas such as the validity of the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and the nature of God. This shocked the orthodox theologians, who sought to discredit Akbar by circulating rumours of his desire to forsake Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion6_146-2"><a href="#cite_note-religion6-146">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>Akbar's effort to evolve a meeting point among the representatives of various religions was not very successful, as each of them attempted to assert the superiority of their respective religions by denouncing other religions. Meanwhile, the debates at the Ibadat Khana grew more acrimonious and, contrary to their purpose of leading to a better understanding among religions, instead led to greater bitterness among them, resulting in the discontinuance of the debates by Akbar in 1582.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra_2007_255_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra_2007_255-151">[150]</a></sup> However, his interaction with various religious theologians had convinced him that despite their differences, all religions had several good practices, which he sought to combine into a new religious movement known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Din-i-Ilahi" title="Din-i-Ilahi">Din-i-Ilahi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silver_rupee_coin_of_Akbar,_from_Lahore_mint.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="562" data-file-width="1074" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Silver_rupee_coin_of_Akbar%2C_from_Lahore_mint.jpg/220px-Silver_rupee_coin_of_Akbar%2C_from_Lahore_mint.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Silver_rupee_coin_of_Akbar%2C_from_Lahore_mint.jpg/330px-Silver_rupee_coin_of_Akbar%2C_from_Lahore_mint.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Silver_rupee_coin_of_Akbar%2C_from_Lahore_mint.jpg/440px-Silver_rupee_coin_of_Akbar%2C_from_Lahore_mint.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silver_rupee_coin_of_Akbar,_from_Lahore_mint.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver square rupee of Akbar, Lahore mint, struck in Aban month of Ilahi</div></div></div>
<p>Some modern scholars claim that Akbar did not initiate a new religion but instead introduced what <a href="/wiki/Oscar_R._G%C3%B3mez" title="Oscar R. GÃ³mez">Dr. Oscar R. GÃ³mez</a> calls the transtheistic outlook from tantric <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhism" title="Tibetan Buddhism">Tibetan Buddhism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup> and that he did not use the word <i>Din-i-Ilahi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup> According to the contemporary events in the Mughal court Akbar was indeed angered by the acts of embezzlement of wealth by many high level Muslim clerics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>The purported Din-i-Ilahi was more of an ethical system and is said to have prohibited lust, sensuality, slander and pride, considering them as sins. Piety, prudence, abstinence and kindness are the core virtues. The soul is encouraged to purify itself through yearning of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLR_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-MLR-157">[156]</a></sup> Celibacy was respected, chastity enforced, the slaughter of animals was forbidden and there were no sacred scriptures or a priestly hierarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> However, a leading Noble of Akbar's court, Aziz Koka, wrote a letter to him from Mecca in 1594 arguing that the discipleship promoted by Akbar amounted to nothing more than a desire on Akbar's part to portray his superiority regarding religious matters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup> To commemorate Din-e-Ilahi, he changed the name of <a href="/wiki/Allahabad" title="Allahabad">Prayag</a> to <a href="/wiki/Allahabad" title="Allahabad">Allahabad</a> (pronounced as <i>ilahabad</i>) in 1583.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has been argued that the theory of Din-i-Ilahi being a new religion was a misconception that arose because of erroneous translations of Abul Fazl's work by later British historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup> However, it is also accepted that the policy of <i>sulh-e-kul</i>, which formed the essence of Din-i-Ilahi, was adopted by Akbar not merely for religious purposes but as a part of general imperial administrative policy. This also formed the basis for Akbar's policy of religious toleration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup> At the time of Akbar's death in 1605 there were no signs of discontent amongst his Muslim subjects, and the impression of even a theologian like Abdu'l Haq was that close ties remained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Habib_1997_96_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Habib_1997_96-164">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_with_Hindus">Relation with Hindus</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_great_Mogul_discoursing_with_a_Humble_Fakir.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2535" data-file-width="1642" decoding="async" height="340" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/The_great_Mogul_discoursing_with_a_Humble_Fakir.jpg/220px-The_great_Mogul_discoursing_with_a_Humble_Fakir.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/The_great_Mogul_discoursing_with_a_Humble_Fakir.jpg/330px-The_great_Mogul_discoursing_with_a_Humble_Fakir.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/The_great_Mogul_discoursing_with_a_Humble_Fakir.jpg/440px-The_great_Mogul_discoursing_with_a_Humble_Fakir.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_great_Mogul_discoursing_with_a_Humble_Fakir.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The great Mogul discoursing with a Humble Fakir</div></div></div>
<p>Akbar decreed that Hindus who had been forced to convert to Islam could reconvert to Hinduism without facing the death penalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup> In his days of tolerance he was so well liked by Hindus that there are numerous references to him, and his eulogies are sung in songs and religious hymns as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>Akbar practised several Hindu customs. He celebrated <a href="/wiki/Diwali" title="Diwali">Diwali</a>, allowed Brahman priests to tie jewelled strings round his wrists by way of blessing, and, following his lead, many of the nobles took to wearing <i>rakhi</i> (protection charms).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-167">[166]</a></sup> He renounced beef and forbade the sale of all meats on certain days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-167">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even his son Jahangir and grandson Shahjahan maintained many of Akbar's concessions, such as the ban on cow slaughter, having only vegetarian dishes on certain days of the week, and drinking only Ganges water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-168">[167]</a></sup> Even as he was in the Punjab, 200 miles away from the Ganges, the water was sealed in large jars and transported to him. He referred to the Ganges water as the "water of immortality."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-168">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_with_Jains">Relation with Jains</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Farrukh_Beg._Akbar%27s_Triumphal_Entry_into_Surat._Akbarnama,_1590-95,_Victoria_and_Albert_Museum,_London.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2439" data-file-width="1531" decoding="async" height="350" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Farrukh_Beg._Akbar%27s_Triumphal_Entry_into_Surat._Akbarnama%2C_1590-95%2C_Victoria_and_Albert_Museum%2C_London.jpg/220px-Farrukh_Beg._Akbar%27s_Triumphal_Entry_into_Surat._Akbarnama%2C_1590-95%2C_Victoria_and_Albert_Museum%2C_London.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Farrukh_Beg._Akbar%27s_Triumphal_Entry_into_Surat._Akbarnama%2C_1590-95%2C_Victoria_and_Albert_Museum%2C_London.jpg/330px-Farrukh_Beg._Akbar%27s_Triumphal_Entry_into_Surat._Akbarnama%2C_1590-95%2C_Victoria_and_Albert_Museum%2C_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Farrukh_Beg._Akbar%27s_Triumphal_Entry_into_Surat._Akbarnama%2C_1590-95%2C_Victoria_and_Albert_Museum%2C_London.jpg/440px-Farrukh_Beg._Akbar%27s_Triumphal_Entry_into_Surat._Akbarnama%2C_1590-95%2C_Victoria_and_Albert_Museum%2C_London.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Farrukh_Beg._Akbar%27s_Triumphal_Entry_into_Surat._Akbarnama,_1590-95,_Victoria_and_Albert_Museum,_London.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Akbar triumphantly enters <a href="/wiki/Surat" title="Surat">Surat</a></div></div></div>
<p>Akbar regularly held discussions with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain" title="Jain">Jain</a> scholars and was also greatly impacted by their teachings. His first encounter with Jain rituals was when he saw a procession of a Jain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shravaka" title="Shravaka">Shravaka</a> named Champa after a six-month-long fast. Impressed by her power and devotion, he invited her <a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">guru</a>, or spiritual teacher, <a href="/wiki/Acharya" title="Acharya">Acharya</a> <a href="/wiki/Hiravijaya" title="Hiravijaya">Hiravijaya</a> Suri to Fatehpur Sikri. Acharya accepted the invitation and began his march towards the Mughal capital from <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanghmitra_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sanghmitra-169">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>Akbar was impressed by the scholastic qualities and character of the Acharya. He held several inter-faith dialogues among philosophers of different religions. The arguments of Jains against eating meat persuaded him to become a vegetarian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup> Akbar also issued many imperial orders that were favourable for Jain interests, such as banning animal slaughter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup> Jain authors also wrote about their experience at the Mughal court in Sanskrit texts that are still largely unknown to Mughal historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_Supreme_Court" title="Indian Supreme Court">Indian Supreme Court</a> has cited examples of co-existence of Jain and Mughal architecture, calling Akbar "the architect of modern India" and that "he had great respect" for Jainism. In 1584, 1592 and 1598, Akbar had declared "Amari Ghosana", which prohibited animal slaughter during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paryushan" title="Paryushan">Paryushan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mahavir_Janma_Kalyanak" title="Mahavir Janma Kalyanak">Mahavira Janma Kalyanak</a>. He removed the Jazia tax from Jain pilgrim places like <a href="/wiki/Palitana" title="Palitana">Palitana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toi_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-toi-173">[172]</a></sup>
Santichandra, disciple of Suri, was sent to the Emperor, who in turn left his disciples Bhanuchandra and Siddhichandra in the court. Akbar again invited Hiravijaya Suri's successor Vijayasena Suri in his court who visited him between 1593 and 1595.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Akbar's religious tolerance was not followed by his son <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a>, who even threatened Akbar's former friend Bhanuchandra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_accounts">Historical accounts</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">Personality</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AkbarHunt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2088" data-file-width="1217" decoding="async" height="292" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/AkbarHunt.jpg/170px-AkbarHunt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/AkbarHunt.jpg/255px-AkbarHunt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/AkbarHunt.jpg/340px-AkbarHunt.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AkbarHunt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Akbar hunting with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asiatic_Cheetah" title="Asiatic Cheetah">cheetahs</a>, c. 1602</div></div></div>
<p>Akbar's reign was chronicled extensively by his court historian <a href="/wiki/Abu%27l-Fazl_ibn_Mubarak" title="Abu'l-Fazl ibn Mubarak">Abul Fazl</a> in the books <i><a href="/wiki/Akbarnama" title="Akbarnama">Akbarnama</a></i> and <i>Ain-i-akbari</i>. Other contemporary sources of Akbar's reign include the works of Badayuni, Shaikhzada Rashidi and Shaikh Ahmed Sirhindi.
</p><p>Akbar was a warrior, emperor, general, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_trainer" title="Animal trainer">animal trainer</a> (reputedly keeping thousands of hunting cheetahs during his reign and training many himself), and theologian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Habib_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Habib-175">[174]</a></sup> Believed to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyslexic" title="Dyslexic">dyslexic</a>, he was read to every day and had a remarkable memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>Akbar was said to have been a wise emperor and a sound judge of character. His son and heir, Jahangir, wrote effusive praise of Akbar's character in his memoirs, and dozens of anecdotes to illustrate his virtues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jahangir_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jahangir-177">[176]</a></sup> According to Jahangir, Akbar was "of the hue of wheat; his eyes and eyebrows were black and his complexion rather dark than fair". <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Antoni_de_Montserrat&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Antoni de Montserrat (page does not exist)">Antoni de Montserrat</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalan_people" title="Catalan people">Catalan</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> who visited his court described him as follows:
</p><p>"One could easily recognize even at first glance that he is King. He has broad shoulders, somewhat bandy legs well-suited for horsemanship, and a light brown complexion. He carries his head bent towards the right shoulder. His forehead is broad and open, his eyes so bright and flashing that they seem like a sea shimmering in the sunlight. His eyelashes are very long. His eyebrows are not strongly marked. His nose is straight and small though not insignificant. His nostrils are widely open as though in derision. Between the left nostril and the upper lip there is a mole. He shaves his beard but wears a moustache. He limps in his left leg though he has never received an injury there."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Portraits_of_Akbar_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Portraits_of_Akbar-178">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>Akbar was not tall but powerfully built and very agile. He was also noted for various acts of courage. One such incident occurred on his way back from Malwa to Agra when Akbar was 19 years of age. Akbar rode alone in advance of his escort and was confronted by a tigress who, along with her cubs, came out from the shrubbery across his path. When the tigress charged the emperor, he was alleged to have dispatched the animal with his sword in a solitary blow. His approaching attendants found the emperor standing quietly by the side of the dead animal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chicago_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-chicago-179">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>Abul Fazl, and even the hostile critic Badayuni, described him as having a commanding personality. He was notable for his command in battle, and, "like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Macedon" title="Alexander III of Macedon">Alexander of Macedon</a>, was always ready to risk his life, regardless of political consequences". He often plunged on his horse into the flooded river during the rainy seasons and safely crossed it. He rarely indulged in cruelty and is said to have been affectionate towards his relatives. He pardoned his brother Hakim, who was a repented rebel. But on rare occasions, he dealt cruelly with offenders, such as his maternal uncle Muazzam and his foster-brother Adham Khan, who was twice <a href="/wiki/Defenestration" title="Defenestration">defenestrated</a> for drawing Akbar's wrath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>He is said to have been extremely moderate in his diet. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ain-e-Akbari" title="Ain-e-Akbari">Ain-e-Akbari</a></i> mentions that during his travels and also while at home, Akbar drank water from the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a> river, which he called 'the water of immortality'. Special people were stationed at Sorun and later <a href="/wiki/Haridwar" title="Haridwar">Haridwar</a> to dispatch water, in sealed jars, to wherever he was stationed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (January 2010)">betterÂ sourceÂ needed</span></a></i>]</sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a>'s memoirs, he was fond of fruits and had little liking for meat, which he stopped eating in his later years.
</p><p>Akbar also once visited <a href="/wiki/Vrindavan" title="Vrindavan">Vrindavan</a>, regarded as the birthplace of <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a>, in the year 1570, and gave permission for four temples to be built by the <a href="/wiki/Gaudiya_Vaishnavism" title="Gaudiya Vaishnavism">Gaudiya Vaishnavas</a>, which were Madana-mohana, Govindaji, Gopinatha and Jugal Kisore.
</p><p>To defend his stance that speech arose from hearing, he carried out a <a href="/wiki/Language_deprivation_experiments" title="Language deprivation experiments">language deprivation experiment</a>, and had children raised in isolation, not allowed to be spoken to, and pointed out that as they grew older, they remained mute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hagiography">Hagiography</span></h3>
<p>During Akbar's reign, the ongoing process of inter-religious discourse and <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a> resulted in a series of religious attributions to him in terms of positions of assimilation, doubt or uncertainty, which he either assisted himself or left unchallenged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hagiography_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-hagiography-183">[182]</a></sup> Such <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hagiographical" title="Hagiographical">hagiographical</a> accounts of Akbar traversed a wide range of denominational and sectarian spaces, including several accounts by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parsi" title="Parsi">Parsis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain" title="Jain">Jains</a> and Jesuit missionaries, apart from contemporary accounts by Brahminical and Muslim orthodoxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[183]</a></sup> Existing sects and denominations, as well as various religious figures who represented popular worship felt they had a claim to him. The diversity of these accounts is attributed to the fact that his reign resulted in the formation of a flexible centralised state accompanied by personal authority and cultural heterogeneity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hagiography_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-hagiography-183">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Akbarn.C4.81ma.2C_the_Book_of_Akbar"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="AkbarnÄma,_the_Book_of_Akbar">AkbarnÄma, the <i>Book of Akbar</i></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Akbarnama" title="Akbarnama">Akbarnama</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AbulFazlPresentingAkbarnama.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4220" data-file-width="2459" decoding="async" height="292" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/AbulFazlPresentingAkbarnama.jpg/170px-AbulFazlPresentingAkbarnama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/AbulFazlPresentingAkbarnama.jpg/255px-AbulFazlPresentingAkbarnama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/AbulFazlPresentingAkbarnama.jpg/340px-AbulFazlPresentingAkbarnama.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AbulFazlPresentingAkbarnama.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Abu%27l-Fazl_ibn_Mubarak" title="Abu'l-Fazl ibn Mubarak">Abu'l-Fazl ibn Mubarak</a> presenting <i><a href="/wiki/Akbarnama" title="Akbarnama">Akbarnama</a></i> to Akbar, Mughal miniature</div></div></div>
<p>The <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">AkbarnÄma</i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa">Ø§Ú©Ø¨Ø± ÙØ§ÙÛ</span>), which literally means <i>Book of Akbar</i>, is an official biographical account of Akbar, the third <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mughal_Emperor" title="Mughal Emperor">Mughal Emperor</a> (r. 1542â1605), written in Persian. It includes vivid and detailed descriptions of his life and times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-art_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-art-185">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>The work was commissioned by Akbar, and written by <a href="/wiki/Abu%27l-Fazl_ibn_Mubarak" title="Abu'l-Fazl ibn Mubarak">Abul Fazl</a>, one of the <i>Nine Jewels</i> (<a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a>: <a href="/wiki/Navaratnas" title="Navaratnas">Navaratnas</a>) of Akbar's royal court. It is stated that the book took seven years to be completed and the original manuscripts contained a number of paintings supporting the texts, and all the paintings represented the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_painting" title="Mughal painting">Mughal school of painting</a>, and work of masters of the imperial workshop, including <a href="/wiki/Basawan" title="Basawan">Basawan</a>, whose use of portraiture in its illustrations was an innovation in <a href="/wiki/Indian_art" title="Indian art">Indian art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-art_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-art-185">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consorts_and_concubines">Consorts and concubines</span></h2>
<p>Akbar's first wife and one of the chief consorts was his cousin, Princess <a href="/wiki/Ruqaiya_Sultan_Begum" title="Ruqaiya Sultan Begum">Ruqaiya Sultan Begum</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sang-E-Meel_Pub_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sang-E-Meel_Pub-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thackston1999p437_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thackston1999p437-6">[5]</a></sup> the only daughter of his paternal uncle, Prince <a href="/wiki/Hindal_Mirza" title="Hindal Mirza">Hindal Mirza</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJahangirThackston199940_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJahangirThackston199940-186">[185]</a></sup> and his wife Sultanam Begum. In 1551, Hindal Mirza died fighting valorously in a battle against Kamran Mirza's forces. Upon hearing the news of his brother's death, Humayun was overwhelmed with grief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erskine_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Erskine-25">[24]</a></sup> Hindal's daughter Ruqaiya married Akbar about the time of his first appointment, at age nine, as governor of <a href="/wiki/Ghazni_Province" title="Ghazni Province">Ghazni Province</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sterling_Publishers_Pvt._Ltd_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sterling_Publishers_Pvt._Ltd-26">[25]</a></sup> Humayun conferred on the imperial couple, all the wealth, army, and adherents of Hindal and Ghazni which one of Hindal's <i><a href="/wiki/Jagir" title="Jagir">jagir</a></i> was given to his nephew, Akbar, who was appointed as its viceroy and was also given the command of his uncle's army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-27">[26]</a></sup> Akbar's marriage with Ruqaiya was solemnized near <a href="/wiki/Jalandhar" title="Jalandhar">Jalandhar</a>, Punjab, when both of them were 14 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly_2000_123,_272_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly_2000_123,_272-28">[27]</a></sup> She was a senior-ranking wife of Akbar. She died childless in January 1626 and was buried next to her father's grave.
</p><p>His second wife was the daughter of Abdullah Khan Mughal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burke1989_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burke1989-187">[186]</a></sup> The marriage took place in 1557 during the siege of <a href="/wiki/Mankot" title="Mankot">Mankot</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bairam_Khan" title="Bairam Khan">Bairam Khan</a> did not approve of this marriage, for Abdullah's sister was married to Akbar's uncle, Prince <a href="/wiki/Kamran_Mirza" title="Kamran Mirza">Kamran Mirza</a>, and so he regarded Abdullah as a partisan of Kamran. He opposed the match until Nasir-al-Mulk made him understand that opposition in such matters was unacceptable. Nasir-al-Mulk arranged an assemblage of pleasure and banquet of joy, and a royal feast was provided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_II190788_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_II190788-188">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>His third wife and one of his three chief consorts was his cousin, <a href="/wiki/Salima_Sultan_Begum" title="Salima Sultan Begum">Salima Sultan Begum</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burke1989_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burke1989-187">[186]</a></sup> the daughter of Nur-ud-din Muhammad Mirza and his wife Gulrukh Begum also known as Gulrang, the daughter of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a>. She was at first betrothed to Bairam Khan by Humayun. After Bairam Khan died in 1561, Akbar married her in the same year. She was the foster mother of Akbar's second son, <a href="/wiki/Murad_Mirza" title="Murad Mirza">Murad Mirza</a>. She held a great influence on Akbar. She was a poetess and was regarded as a remarkable woman being a poetess, lover of books and actively played role in the politics of the Mughal court during Akbar's and Jahangir's reigns. She is regarded aa the senior-most wife of Akbar. She died childless on 2 January 1613.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJahangirThackston1999140_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJahangirThackston1999140-189">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>Akbar's fourth and favourite wife <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farishta_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-farishta-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-indiatoday_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-indiatoday-192">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELal1980322_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELal1980322-193">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qa_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Qa-194">[193]</a></sup> was the <a href="/wiki/Mariam-uz-Zamani" title="Mariam-uz-Zamani">Mariam-uz-Zamani</a>, commonly known by misnomer Jodha Bai, whom he married in the year 1562. She was the daughter of the ruler of Amer, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raja_Bharmal" title="Raja Bharmal">Raja Bharmal</a> and was by birth of <a href="/wiki/Rajput_clans" title="Rajput clans">Rajput caste</a>.
