She was his fourth wife and became one of his chief consorts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[194]</a></sup> She gradually became his most influential wife<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qa_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-Qa-194">[193]</a></sup> and subsequently is the only wife buried close to him. She was bestowed with the name 'Wali Nimat Begum' (Blessings/Gift of God) by Akbar shortly after her marriage. She was a beautiful woman said to possess uncommon beauty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup> This marriage took place when Akbar was on his way back from <a href="/wiki/Ajmer" title="Ajmer">Ajmer</a> after offering prayers to the tomb of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moinuddin_Chishti" title="Moinuddin Chishti">Moinuddin Chishti</a>. Raja Bharmal had conveyed to Akbar that he was being harassed by his brother-in-law Sharif-ud-din Mirza (the Mughal <i><a href="/wiki/Hakim_(title)" title="Hakim (title)">hakim</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Mewat" title="Mewat">Mewat</a>). Akbar insisted that the Raja should submit to him personally, it was also suggested that his daughter should be married to him as a sign of complete submission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_II1907240â243_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_II1907240â243-197">[196]</a></sup> Her marriage is considered one of the most important events of the Mughal Empire. She became his first wife to honour the royal mansion with an heir. In the year 1564, she gave birth to twins named Mirza Hassan and Mirza Hussain. In the year 1569, she was honoured with the title of 'Mariam-uz-Zamani' after giving birth to their third son named Prince Salim (the future emperor <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a>), the heir to the throne. She was also the foster mother of Akbar's favourite son, <a href="/wiki/Daniyal_Mirza" title="Daniyal Mirza">Daniyal Mirza</a>. She commanded a high rank in the imperial harem and held many powers in the harem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[197]</a></sup> She was an intelligent woman<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ain-i-Akbari_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ain-i-Akbari-199">[198]</a></sup>  who held a significant influence in Akbar's court and is known as the prime driving force for Akbar's promotion of secularism and religious neutrality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lal9_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-lal9-200">[199]</a></sup> She was a smart woman who established international trade in the Mughal Empire and is regarded as the most adventurous and fearsome businesswoman of her time by contemporaries. She died on 19 May 1623 in Agra and was buried close to her husband in Sikandra. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJahangirThackston1999397_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJahangirThackston1999397-201">[200]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AkbarMariamuzZamani.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1446" data-file-width="1001" decoding="async" height="318" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/AkbarMariamuzZamani.jpg/220px-AkbarMariamuzZamani.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/AkbarMariamuzZamani.jpg/330px-AkbarMariamuzZamani.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/AkbarMariamuzZamani.jpg/440px-AkbarMariamuzZamani.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AkbarMariamuzZamani.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Jalaluddin Muhammad Akbar with <a href="/wiki/Mariam-uz-Zamani" title="Mariam-uz-Zamani">Mariam Zamani Begum</a>, drawn as per Akbar's description.</div></div></div>
<p>In the year 1562, Akbar married the former wife of Abdul Wasi, the son of Shaikh Bada, lord of Agra. Akbar was enamored with her beauty, and ordered Abdul Wasi to divorce her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[201]</a></sup> Another of his wives was Gauhar-un-Nissa Begum, the daughter of Shaikh Muhammad Bakhtiyar and the sister of Shaikh Jamal Bakhtiyar. Their dynasty was called Din Laqab and had been living for a long time in Chandwar and Jalesar near Agra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[202]</a></sup> He married the daughter of Jagmal Rathore, son of Rao Viramde of <a href="/wiki/Merta_City" title="Merta City">Merta</a> in 1562.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>His next marriage took place in 1564 to the daughter of Miran Mubarak Shah, the ruler of <a href="/wiki/Khandesh" title="Khandesh">Khandesh</a>. In 1564, he sent presents to the court with a request that his daughter be married to Akbar. Miran's request acceded and an order was issued. Itimad Khan was sent with Miran's ambassadors, and when he came near the fort of Asir, which was Miran's residence. Miran welcomed Itimad with honor and despatched his daughter with Itimad. A large number of nobles accompanied her. The marriage took place in September 1564 when she reached Akbar's court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_II1907352_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_II1907352-205">[204]</a></sup> As a dowry, Mubarak Shah ceded Bijagarh and Handia to his imperial son-in-law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup>
</p><p>He married another Rajput princess in 1570, Raj Kunwari, daughter of Kanha, the brother of Rai Kalyan Mal, the ruler of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bikanir" title="Bikanir">Bikanir</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-207">[206]</a></sup> The marriage took place in 1570 when Akbar came to this part of the country. Kalyan made a homage to Akbar and requested that his brother's daughter be married to him. Akbar accepted his proposal, and the marriage was arranged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[207]</a></sup> He also married Bhanmati, daughter of Bhim Raj, another brother of Rai Kalyan Mal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-207">[206]</a></sup> He also married Nathi Bai, daughter of Rawal Har Rai, the ruler of <a href="/wiki/Jaisalmer" title="Jaisalmer">Jaisalmer</a> in 1570.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_II1907518_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_II1907518-209">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[210]</a></sup> Rawal had sent a request that his daughter be married to Akbar. The proposal was accepted by Akbar. Raja Bhagwan Das was despatched on this service. The marriage ceremony took place after Akbar's return from <a href="/wiki/Nagor" title="Nagor">Nagor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_II1907518â519_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_II1907518â519-212">[211]</a></sup> She was the mother of Princess Mahi Begum, who died on 8 April 1577.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_II1907283_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_II1907283-213">[212]</a></sup> In 1570, Narhardas, a grandson of Rao Viramde of <a href="/wiki/Merta_City" title="Merta City">Merta</a>, married his sister, Puram Bai, to Akbar in return for Akbar's support of Keshodas's claims on Merta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another of his wives was Bhakkari Begum, the daughter of Sultan Mahmud of Bhakkar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[215]</a></sup> On 2 July 1572, Akbar's envoy I'timad Khan reached Mahmud's court to escort his daughter to Akbar. Itimad Khan brought with him for Sultan Mahmud an elegant dress of honor, a bejeweled scimitar belt, a horse with a saddle and reins, and four elephants. Mahmud celebrated the occasion by holding extravagant feasts for fifteen days. On the day of the wedding, the festivities reached their zenith, and the ulema, saints, and nobles were adequately honored with rewards. Mahmud offered 30,000 rupees in cash and kind to Itimad Khan and farewelled his daughter with a grand dowry and an impressive entourage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[216]</a></sup> She came to Ajmer and waited upon Akbar. The gifts of Sultan Mahmud, carried by the delegation were presented to the ladies of the imperial harem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[217]</a></sup>
</p><p>His ninth wife was Qasima Banu Begum,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burke1989_187-2"><a href="#cite_note-Burke1989-187">[186]</a></sup> the daughter of Arab Shah. The marriage took place in 1575. A great feast was given, and the high officers and other pillars of the state were present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_III1907167â168_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_III1907167â168-219">[218]</a></sup> In 1577, the Rawal Askaran of <a href="/wiki/Dungarpur_State" title="Dungarpur State">Dungarpur State</a> petitioned a request that his daughter might be married to Akbar. Akbar had regard for his loyalty and granted his request.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_III1907278_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_III1907278-220">[219]</a></sup> Rai Loukaran and Rajah Birbar, servants of the Rajah were sent from Dihalpur to do the honor of conveying his daughter. The two delivered the lady at Akbar's court where the marriage took place on 12 July 1577.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_III1907295_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_III1907295-221">[220]</a></sup>
</p><p>His eleventh wife was Bibi Daulat Shad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burke1989_187-3"><a href="#cite_note-Burke1989-187">[186]</a></sup> She was the mother of Princess Shakr-un-Nissa Begum, and Princess <a href="/wiki/Aram_Banu_Begum" title="Aram Banu Begum">Aram Banu Begum</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJahangirThackston199939_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJahangirThackston199939-222">[221]</a></sup> born on 22 December 1584.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_III1907661_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_III1907661-224">[223]</a></sup> His next wife was the daughter of Shams Chak, a Kashmiri. The marriage took place on 3 November 1592. Shams belonged to the great men of the country and had long cherished this wish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_III1907958_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_III1907958-225">[224]</a></sup> In 1593, he married the daughter of Qazi Isa and the cousin of Najib Khan. Najib told Akbar that his uncle had made his daughter a present for him. Akbar accepted his representation and on 3 July 1593, he visited Najib Khan's house and married Qazi Isa's daughter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_III1907985_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeveridge_Volume_III1907985-226">[225]</a></sup>
</p><p>At some point, Akbar took into his <a href="/wiki/Harem" title="Harem">harem</a> Rukmavati, a daughter of Rao <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maldev_Rathore" title="Maldev Rathore">Maldev</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jodhpur_State" title="Jodhpur State">Marwar</a> by his mistress, Tipu Gudi. This was a <i>dolo</i> union as opposed to formal marriage, representing the bride's lower status in her father's household, and serving as an expression of vassalage to an overlord. The dating of this event is not recorded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sreenivasan2006_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sreenivasan2006-227">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandra1993_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chandra1993-228">[227]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gate_of_the_Tomb_of_Akbar_at_Sikandra,_Agra,_India,_1795.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="352" data-file-width="424" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Gate_of_the_Tomb_of_Akbar_at_Sikandra%2C_Agra%2C_India%2C_1795.jpg/220px-Gate_of_the_Tomb_of_Akbar_at_Sikandra%2C_Agra%2C_India%2C_1795.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Gate_of_the_Tomb_of_Akbar_at_Sikandra%2C_Agra%2C_India%2C_1795.jpg/330px-Gate_of_the_Tomb_of_Akbar_at_Sikandra%2C_Agra%2C_India%2C_1795.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1d/Gate_of_the_Tomb_of_Akbar_at_Sikandra%2C_Agra%2C_India%2C_1795.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gate_of_the_Tomb_of_Akbar_at_Sikandra,_Agra,_India,_1795.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gate of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Akbar_the_Great" title="Tomb of Akbar the Great">Akbar's mausoleum</a> at Sikandra, Agra, 1795</div></div></div>
<p>On 3 October 1605, Akbar fell ill from an attack of <a href="/wiki/Dysentery" title="Dysentery">dysentery</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[228]</a></sup> from which he never recovered. He is believed to have died on 27 October 1605. He was buried at <a href="/wiki/Akbar%27s_tomb" title="Akbar's tomb">his mausoleum</a> in Sikandra, Agra which lies a kilometer next to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mariam%27s_Tomb" title="Mariam's Tomb">tomb of Mariam-uz-Zamani</a>, his favourite and chief consort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[229]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farishta_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-farishta-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jlmehta_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-jlmehta-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<p>Akbar left a rich legacy both for the Mughal Empire as well as the Indian subcontinent in general. He firmly entrenched the authority of the Mughal Empire in India and beyond, after it had been threatened by the Afghans during his father's reign,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[230]</a></sup> establishing its military and diplomatic superiority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[231]</a></sup> During his reign, the nature of the state changed to a secular and liberal one, with emphasis on cultural integration. He also introduced several far-sighted social reforms, including prohibiting <i><a href="/wiki/Sati_(practice)" title="Sati (practice)">sati</a></i>, legalizing widow remarriage, and raising the age of marriage.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">Folk tales</a> revolving around him and <a href="/wiki/Birbal" title="Birbal">Birbal</a>, one of his <i>navratnas</i>, are popular in India. He and his Hindu wife, <a href="/wiki/Mariam-uz-Zamani" title="Mariam-uz-Zamani">Mariam-uz-Zamani</a>, in the popular culture known as <i> 'Jodha Bai' </i> are widely popular as the latter is believed to have been the prime inspiration and driving force for Akbar's promotion of secularism and universal benevolence(Sulh-i-Qul).
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Bhavishya_Purana" title="Bhavishya Purana">Bhavishya Purana</a></i> is a minor <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Purana" title="Purana">Purana</a></i> that depicts the various Hindu holy days and includes a section devoted to the various dynasties that ruled India, dating its oldest portion to 500 CE and newest to the 18th century. It contains a story about Akbar in which he is compared to the other Mughal rulers. The section called "Akbar Bahshaha Varnan", written in Sanskrit describes his birth as a "<a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a>" of a sage who immolated himself on seeing the first Mughal ruler Babur, who is described as the "cruel king of Mlecchas (Muslims)". In this text it is stated that Akbar "was a miraculous child" and that he would not follow the previous "violent ways" of the Mughals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khanna,_Culture_of_Medieval_India_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-Khanna,_Culture_of_Medieval_India-233">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Review_colonial_record_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Review_colonial_record-234">[233]</a></sup>
</p><p>Citing Akbar's melding of the disparate 'fiefdoms' of India into the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> as well as the lasting legacy of "pluralism and tolerance" that "underlies the values of the modern republic of India", <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time magazine</a> included his name in its list of top 25 world leaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-time-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, his legacy is explicitly negative in <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> for the same reasons. Historian <a href="/wiki/Mubarak_Ali" title="Mubarak Ali">Mubarak Ali</a>, while studying the image of Akbar in Pakistani textbooks, observes that Akbar "is conveniently ignored and not mentioned in any school textbook from class one to matriculation", as opposed to the omnipresence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Aurangzeb" title="Emperor Aurangzeb">emperor Aurangzeb</a>. He quotes historian <a href="/wiki/Ishtiaq_Hussain_Qureshi" title="Ishtiaq Hussain Qureshi">Ishtiaq Hussain Qureshi</a>, who said that, due to his religious tolerance, "Akbar had so weakened Islam through his policies that it could not be restored to its dominant position in the affairs." A common thread among Pakistani historians is to blame Akbar's <a href="/wiki/Rajput" title="Rajput">Rajput</a> policy. In a conclusion, after analyzing many textbooks, Mubarak Ali says that "Akbar is criticized for bringing Muslims and Hindus together as one nation and putting the separate identity of the Muslims in danger. This policy of Akbar contradicts the <a href="/wiki/Two-nation_theory" title="Two-nation theory">theory of Two-Nation</a> and therefore makes him an unpopular figure in Pakistan."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[234]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Issue">Issue</span></h2>
<p>Akbar's sons were:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mariam-uz-Zamani#Birth_of_Twins" title="Mariam-uz-Zamani">Hassan Mirza</a> ( <abbr title="birth">b.</abbr> 19 October 1564; <abbr title="death">d.</abbr> 5 November 1564) (twin with Hussain Mirza)âwith <a href="/wiki/Mariam-uz-Zamani" title="Mariam-uz-Zamani">Mariam-uz-Zamani Begum</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELal1980133_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELal1980133-236">[235]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mariam-uz-Zamani#Birth_of_Twins" title="Mariam-uz-Zamani">Hussain Mirza</a> ( <abbr title="birth">b.</abbr> 19 October 1564; <abbr title="death">d.</abbr> 29 October 1564) (twin with Hassan Mirza)âwith Mariam-uz-Zamani Begum<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELal1980133_236-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELal1980133-236">[235]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Shahzada Salim</a> ( <abbr title="birth">b.</abbr> 31 August 1569; <abbr title="death">d.</abbr> 28 October 1627)âwith Mariam-uz-Zamani BegumâHe succeeded Akbar to the throne.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Murad_Mirza_(son_of_Akbar)" title="Murad Mirza (son of Akbar)">Murad Mirza</a> ( <abbr title="birth">b.</abbr> 15 June 1570; <abbr title="death">d.</abbr> 12 May 1599)âwith Mariam-uz-Zamani Begum or a concubineâFostered by <a href="/wiki/Salima_Sultan_Begum" title="Salima Sultan Begum">Salima Sultan Begum</a> for the first few years and then returned to his mother's care before 1575.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Daniyal_Mirza" title="Daniyal Mirza">Daniyal Mirza</a> ( <abbr title="birth">b.</abbr> 11 September 1572; <abbr title="death">d.</abbr> 19 March 1605 )â with a concubine â Fostered by Mariam-uz-Zamani Begum.</li>
<li>Shahzada Khusrao ( <abbr title="death">d.</abbr> infancy)âwith a niece Rai Kalyan Mal of <a href="/wiki/Bikaner" title="Bikaner">Bikaner</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ul>
<p>Akbar's daughters were:
</p>
<ul><li>Fatima Banu Begum ( <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">â1562</span>; <abbr title="death">d.</abbr> infancy)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[237]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shahzada_Khanam" title="Shahzada Khanam">Shahzada Khanam</a> ( <abbr title="birth">b.</abbr> 21 November 1569)âwith Bibi SalimaâFostered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mariam_Makani" title="Mariam Makani">Mariam Makani</a>âMarried to Muzaffar Hussain Mirza, <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurid Prince</a>.</li>
<li>Mahi Begum ( <abbr title="death">d.</abbr> 7 April 1577)âwith Nathi Bai</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shakr-un-Nissa_Begum" title="Shakr-un-Nissa Begum">Shakr-un-Nissa Begum</a> (<abbr title="death">d.</abbr> 1 January 1653)âwith Bibi Daulat ShadâMarried to Shahrukh Mirza.</li>
<li>Firoze Khannum ( <abbr title="birth">b.</abbr> 1575)âwith a concubine âFostered by Mariam-uz-Zamani Begum<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aram_Banu_Begum" title="Aram Banu Begum">Aram Banu Begum</a> ( <abbr title="birth">b.</abbr> 22 December 1584; <abbr title="death">d.</abbr> 17 June 1624)âwith Bibi Daulat Shad</li></ul>
<p>He had also adopted several children includingÂ :
</p>
<ul><li>Kishnavati Bai ( <abbr title="death">d.</abbr> August 1609)âdaughter of Sekhavat Kachvahi Durjan Sal. Akbar took her as his own and had her married to <a href="/wiki/Sur_Singh" title="Sur Singh">Sawai Raja Sur Singh</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jodhpur_State" title="Jodhpur State">Marwar</a>. She became the mother of <a href="/wiki/Gaj_Singh_of_Marwar" title="Gaj Singh of Marwar">Maharaja Gaj Singh</a> of Marwar and Manbhavati Bai, wife of <a href="/wiki/Parviz_Mirza" title="Parviz Mirza">Parviz Mirza</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[238]</a></sup></li></ul>

<dl><dt>Films and television</dt></dl>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Shahenshah_Akbar" title="Shahenshah Akbar">Shahenshah Akbar</a></i> is a 1943 Indian <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a>-language film about the emperor, directed by G. R. Sethi.</li>
<li>Akbar was portrayed in the award-winning 1960 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Hindi movie</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Mughal-e-Azam" title="Mughal-e-Azam">Mughal-e-Azam</a></i> (The great Mughal), in which his character was played by <a href="/wiki/Prithviraj_Kapoor" title="Prithviraj Kapoor">Prithviraj Kapoor</a>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India" title="Government of India">Government of India</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Films_Division" title="Films Division">Films Division</a> produced <i>Akbar</i>, a documentary film about the emperor, in 1967 which was directed by Shanti S. Varma. It won the <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Award_for_Best_Educational/Motivational/Instructional_Film" title="National Film Award for Best Educational/Motivational/Instructional Film">National Film Award for Best Educational/Motivational/Instructional Film</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Om_Shivpuri" title="Om Shivpuri">Om Shivpuri</a> played Akbar in 1978 movie <i><a href="/wiki/Bhakti_Mein_Shakti" title="Bhakti Mein Shakti">Bhakti Mein Shakti</a></i>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Akbar_Salim_Anarkali" title="Akbar Salim Anarkali">Akbar Saleem Anarkali</a> is a 1979 Indian <a href="/wiki/Telugu_language" title="Telugu language">Telugu</a>-language film about the <a href="/wiki/Anarkali" title="Anarkali">Anarkali legend</a> directed by <a href="/wiki/N._T._Rama_Rao" title="N. T. Rama Rao">N. T. Rama Rao</a>, with Rao also portraying the role of Akbar.</li>
<li>Akbar was portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Amjad_Khan_(actor)" title="Amjad Khan (actor)">Amjad Khan</a> in 1979 movie <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meera" title="Meera">Meera</a></i>.</li>
<li>Akbar was portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Hrithik_Roshan" title="Hrithik Roshan">Hrithik Roshan</a> in the 2008 Bollywood film <i><a href="/wiki/Jodhaa_Akbar" title="Jodhaa Akbar">Jodhaa Akbar</a></i>.</li>
<li>Akbar and Birbal were portrayed in the Hindi series <i>Akbar-Birbal</i> aired on <a href="/wiki/Zee_TV" title="Zee TV">Zee TV</a> in the late 1990s where Akbar's role was played by <a href="/wiki/Vikram_Gokhale" title="Vikram Gokhale">Vikram Gokhale</a>.</li>
<li>A television series, called <i>Akbar the Great</i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Akbar_Khan_(director)" title="Akbar Khan (director)">Akbar Khan</a> was aired on <a href="/wiki/DD_National" title="DD National">DD National</a> in the 1990s.</li>
<li>Since 2013â2015, a television series, called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jodha_Akbar_(TV_series)" title="Jodha Akbar (TV series)">Jodha Akbar</a></i> aired on Zee TV, in which the role of Akbar was played by actor <a href="/wiki/Rajat_Tokas" title="Rajat Tokas">Rajat Tokas</a>.</li>
<li>In the Motu Patlu episode "Motu Akbar The Great" John fools Motu into believing he's playing as Akbar in a Hit Film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[239]</a></sup></li>
<li>Akbar was portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Uday_Tikekar" title="Uday Tikekar">Uday Tikekar</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EPIC_(TV_channel)" title="EPIC (TV channel)">EPIC</a> channel's critically acclaimed historical drama <i><a href="/wiki/Siyaasat" title="Siyaasat">Siyaasat</a></i> (based on the novel <i>The Twentieth Wife</i>).</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Sony_Entertainment_Television" title="Sony Entertainment Television">Sony TV</a>'s historical drama <i><a href="/wiki/Bharat_Ka_Veer_Putra_%E2%80%93_Maharana_Pratap" title="Bharat Ka Veer Putra â Maharana Pratap">Bharat Ka Veer Putra â Maharana Pratap</a></i>, Akbar was at first portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Krip_Suri" title="Krip Suri">Krip Suri</a> and later by <a href="/wiki/Avinesh_Rekhi" title="Avinesh Rekhi">Avinesh Rekhi</a>.</li>
<li>Akbar is portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Kiku_Sharda" title="Kiku Sharda">Kiku Sharda</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BIG_Magic" title="BIG Magic">BIG Magic</a>'s sitcom <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akbar_Birbal" title="Akbar Birbal">Akbar Birbal</a></i>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saurabh_Raj_Jain" title="Saurabh Raj Jain">Saurabh Raj Jain</a> portrayed Akbar in the follow up sitcom by BIG Magic, <i><a href="/wiki/Hazir_Jawab_Birbal" title="Hazir Jawab Birbal">Hazir Jawab Birbal</a></i>.</li>
<li>Abhishek Nigam portrayed Akbar in BIG MAGIC's historical drama <i>Akbar â Rakht Se Takht Tak Ka Safar</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Iqbal_Khan" title="Mohammed Iqbal Khan">Mohammed Iqbal Khan</a> played the role of Akbar in ABP News' documentary series, <a href="/wiki/Bharatvarsh_(TV_series)" title="Bharatvarsh (TV series)">Bharatvarsh</a>.</li>
<li>Akbar Rakht Se Takht Ka Safar is a 2017 Indian drama television series tracing Akbar's journey to the Mughal throne. Abhishek Nigam portrays the role of Akbar.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shahbaz_Khan_(actor)" title="Shahbaz Khan (actor)">Shahbaz Khan</a> played the role of Akbar in the Colors television show <a href="/wiki/Dastaan-E-Mohabbat_Salim_Anarkali" title="Dastaan-E-Mohabbat Salim Anarkali">Dastaan-E-Mohabbat Salim Anarkali</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ali_Asgar_(actor)" title="Ali Asgar (actor)">Ali Asgar</a> portrayed the emperor in the 2020 Indian comedy television series, <i><a href="/wiki/Akbar_Ka_Bal_Birbal" title="Akbar Ka Bal Birbal">Akbar Ka Bal Birbal</a></i>.</li></ul>
<dl><dt>Fiction</dt></dl>
<ul><li>Akbar is a principal character in <a href="/wiki/Indu_Sundaresan" title="Indu Sundaresan">Indu Sundaresan</a>'s award-winning novel <i>The Twentieth Wife</i> (2002) as well as in its sequel <i>The Feast of Roses</i> (2003).</li>
<li>A fictionalised Akbar plays an important supporting role in <a href="/wiki/Kim_Stanley_Robinson" title="Kim Stanley Robinson">Kim Stanley Robinson</a>'s 2002 novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Years_of_Rice_and_Salt" title="The Years of Rice and Salt">The Years of Rice and Salt</a></i>.</li>
<li>Akbar is also a major character in <a href="/wiki/Salman_Rushdie" title="Salman Rushdie">Salman Rushdie</a>'s 2008 novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Enchantress_of_Florence" title="The Enchantress of Florence">The Enchantress of Florence</a></i>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bertrice_Small" title="Bertrice Small">Bertrice Small</a> is known for incorporating historical figures as primary characters in her romance novels, and Akbar is no exception. He is a prominent figure in two of her novels, and mentioned several times in a third, which takes place after his death. In <i>This Heart of Mine</i> the heroine becomes Akbar's fortieth "wife" for a time, while <i>Wild Jasmine</i> and <i>Darling Jasmine</i> centre around the life of his half-British daughter, Yasaman Kama Begum (alias Jasmine).</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Kunal_Basu" title="Kunal Basu">Kunal Basu</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Miniaturist_(Kunal_Basu_novel)" title="The Miniaturist (Kunal Basu novel)">The Miniaturist</a></i>, the story revolves around a young painter during Akbar's time who paints his own version of the <i>Akbarnamu</i></li>
<li>Akbar is mentioned as 'Raja Baadshah' in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chhattisgarhi" title="Chhattisgarhi">Chhattisgarhi</a> <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folktale</a> of "<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mohna_de_gori_kayina&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mohna de gori kayina (page does not exist)">Mohna de gori kayina</a>"</li>
<li>Akbar is the main character in <i><a href="/wiki/Empire_of_the_Moghul" title="Empire of the Moghul">Empire of the Moghul</a>: Ruler of the World</i> by <a href="/wiki/Alex_Rutherford" title="Alex Rutherford">Alex Rutherford</a>, the third book in a sextet based on the six great Mughal Emperors of the Mughal Dynasty.</li></ul>
<dl><dt>Video games</dt></dl>
<ul><li>Akbar is featured in the video game <i><a href="/wiki/Sid_Meier" title="Sid Meier">Sid Meier</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Civilization_IV:_Beyond_the_Sword" title="Civilization IV: Beyond the Sword">Civilization IV: Beyond the Sword</a></i> as a "great general" available in the game.</li>
<li>Akbar is also the AI Personality of India in the renowned game <i><a href="/wiki/Age_of_Empires_III:_The_Asian_Dynasties" title="Age of Empires III: The Asian Dynasties">Age of Empires III: The Asian Dynasties</a></i>.</li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-birth-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-birth_5-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-birth_5-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-birth_5-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Official sources, such as contemporary biographer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu%27l-Fazl" title="Abu'l-Fazl">Abu'l-Fazl</a>, record Akbar's birth name and date as Jalal ud-din Muhammad Akbar and 15 October 1542 . However, based on recollections of Humayun's personal attendant Jauhar, historian <a href="/wiki/Vincent_Arthur_Smith" title="Vincent Arthur Smith">Vincent Arthur Smith</a> holds that Akbar was born on 23 November 1542 (the fourteenth day of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sha%27aban" title="Sha'aban">Sha'aban</a>, which had a full moon) and was originally named Badr ud-din ("The full moon of religion"). According to Smith, the recorded date of birth was changed at the time of Akbar's circumcision ceremony in March 1546 in order to throw off astrologers and sorcerers, and the name accordingly changed to Jalal ud-din ("Splendour of Religion")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-Thackston1999p437-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Thackston1999p437_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Thackston1999p437_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Thackston1999p437_6-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFJahangir,_Emperor_of_Hindustan1999">Jahangir, Emperor of Hindustan (1999). <i>The Jahangirnama: Memoirs of Jahangir, Emperor of India</i>. Translated by <a href="/wiki/Wheeler_Thackston" title="Wheeler Thackston">Thackston, Wheeler M.</a> Oxford University Press. p.Â 437. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>Â <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-512718-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-512718-8"><bdi>978-0-19-512718-8</bdi></a>. <q>Ruqayya-Sultan Begam, the daughter of Mirza Hindal and wife of His Majesty Arsh-Ashyani [Akbar], had passed away in Akbarabad. She was His Majesty's chief wife. Since she did not have children, when Shahjahan was born His Majesty Arsh-Ashyani entrusted that "unique pearl of the caliphate" to the begam's care, and she undertook to raise the prince. She departed this life at the age of eighty-four.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Jahangirnama%3A+Memoirs+of+Jahangir%2C+Emperor+of+India&amp;rft.pages=437&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-512718-8&amp;rft.au=Jahangir%2C+Emperor+of+Hindustan&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAkbar"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-farishta-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-farishta_8-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-farishta_8-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-farishta_8-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFHindu_Shah1595â1612">Hindu Shah, Muhammad Qasim (1595â1612). <i>Gulshan-I-Ibrahimi</i>. Vol.Â 2. p.Â 223. <q>Akbur, after this conquest, made pilgrimage to Khwaja Moyin-ood-Deen Chishty at Ajmere and returned to Agra; from whence he proceeded to visit the venerable Sheikh Sulim Chishty, in the village of Seekry. As all the king's children had hitherto died, he solicited the Sheikh's prayers, who consoled him, by assuring him he would soon have a son, who would live to a good old age. Shortly after, his favourite sooltana, being then pregnant, on Wednesday the 17th of Rubbee-ool-Awul, in the year 997 was delivered of a son, who was called Sulim.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Gulshan-I-Ibrahimi&amp;rft.pages=223&amp;rft.date=1595%2F1612&amp;rft.aulast=Hindu+Shah&amp;rft.aufirst=Muhammad+Qasim&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAkbar"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-jlmehta-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-jlmehta_9-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-jlmehta_9-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMehta1981">Mehta, J.L. (1981). <i>Advance Study in the history of Medieval India:Mughal Empire</i>. Vol.Â II. Sterling Publisher Private Limited. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>Â <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/8120704320" title="Special:BookSources/8120704320"><bdi>8120704320</bdi></a>. <q>Bihari Mal gave rich dowry to his daughter and sent his son Bhagwan Das with a contingent of Rajput soldiers to escort his newly married sister to Agra as per Hindu custom. Akbar was deeply impressed by the highly dignified, sincere and princely conduct of his Rajput relations. He took Man Singh, the youthful son of Bhagwant Das into the royal service. Akbar was fascinated by the charm and accomplishments of his Rajput wife; he developed real love for her and raised her to the status of chief queen. She came to exercise profound impact on socio-cultural environment of the entire royal household and changed the lifestyle of Akbar. Salim (later Jahangir), heir to the throne, was born of this wedlock on 30th August, 1569.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Advance+Study+in+the+history+of+Medieval+India%3AMughal+Empire&amp;rft.pub=Sterling+Publisher+Private+Limited&amp;rft.date=1981&amp;rft.isbn=8120704320&amp;rft.aulast=Mehta&amp;rft.aufirst=J.L.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAkbar"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-195"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-195">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMehta1981">Mehta, J.L. Sterling Publisher Private Limited. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>Â <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/8120704320" title="Special:BookSources/8120704320"><bdi>8120704320</bdi></a>. <q>Bihari Mal gave rich dowry to his daughter and sent his son Bhagwan Das with a contingent of Rajput soldiers to escort his newly married sister to Agra as per Rajput custom. Akbar was deeply impressed by the highly dignified, sincere, and princely conduct of his Rajput relations. He took Man Singh, the youthful son of Bhagwant Das into the royal service. Akbar was fascinated by the charm and accomplishments of his Rajput wife; he developed real love for her and raised her to the status of chief queen. She came to exercise a profound impact on the socio-cultural environment of the entire royal household and changed the lifestyle of Akbar. Salim (later Jahangir), the heir to the throne, was born of this wedlock on 30th August 1569.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Advance+Study+in+the+history+of+Medieval+India&amp;rft.pub=Sterling+Publisher+Private+Limited&amp;rft.date=1981&amp;rft.isbn=8120704320&amp;rft.aulast=Mehta&amp;rft.aufirst=J.L.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAkbar"></span></span>
